â -"My own view. is that a few irre

Special Dispatch to The Call.
: HAVANA. May 17.â General Brooke
I submitted- to General Gomez to-day a
j new distribution decree. It proved
, satisfactory to Gomez. Every effort
j is being made to avoid another hitch.
: Brooke's decree and Gomez's manifesto
will be Issued to-morrow simultane
; ously
Gomez states that he will not take
an active part In the distribution of
the money, which will be made by the
American commissioners from the lists
submitted.
I have had many interviews with
Cuban soldiers. All are willing to give
up their arms. The feeling is strong
among both officers and soldiers that
they should be given work by the
United States. They assert that a large
majority of the offices in Cuba are now
held by men who were not active in
the war.- They think a protest will be
made on that ground.

SAN FRANCISCO, THURSDAY, MAY 18, 1899.
L. J. ROSE
TAKES HIS
OWN LIFE
Suicide of the Well-Kown
Viticulturist and
Turfman.
â â¦ â .
HAD LOST A FORTUNE
4
Failing to Negotiate a Loan the
Former Millionaire Takes
Former Millionaire Takes
Poison.
â â
Special Dispatch to The Call.
LOS ANGELES, May 17.â Worried
by numerous debts, L. J. Rose,
the pioneer viticulturist of Cali
fornia and one of the best-known
turfmen in the United States, endel
his life to-day. Rose swallowed a
large dose of morphine while stand
ing in the chicken yard at the rear
of his mansion at the corner of
Fourth street and Grand avenue. In
spite of the efforts of two physicians
he died at 11:30 o'clock this forenoon,
one hour after he was found.
A few days ago Mr. Rose went to
San Francisco to obtain a loan. He
was unsuccessful and returned to Los
Angeles. Yesterday morning he went
to Ventura to borrow money. The sum
he wanted was a large one and he could
not obtain it. Mr. Rose returned from
Ventura last night, apparently in his
usual health and spirits. Developments
show, however, that he determined last
night to destroy himself. Mr. Rose
wrote a letter to- his wife in which he
said he was "swamped financially"
and would not be alive much longer.
"You will find my remains in the
chicken yard," were the concluding
words of the letter.
This letter was found by a member
of Mr. Rose's household soon after 10
o'clock this morning and search was
at once instituted for Mr. Ruse, who
was found lying unconscious in the
chicken yard.
Mr. Rose was carried to his bedroom
and Drs. W. W. Hitchcock and David
son were hurriedly summoned. The
physicians worked over Mr. Rose for
an hour, but did not "succeed in reviv
ing him. Several members of the fam
ily were at his bedside when he died.
Coroner Holland, who was notified of
the suicide at noon, went to the house
and viewed the remains. Mr. Rose's
relatives were anxious to have the , In
quest held immediately, and the Coro
ner said he would hold it this evening.
Mrs. Rose is an invalid and the news
of her husand's death was a severe
blow. She Is prostrated by grief.
In the history of California and of
racetracks there was no better-known
man than Mr. Rose. To him Is due the
credit of developing the wine Industry
of Southern California. He came to
Los Angeles in IS6O and acquired the
Sunny Slope ranch near San Gabriel.
Under his management the ranch be
came famous as a vineyard and the
choicest wines were made on the place.
The dead man was a great lover of
horses and he had' a large stable.
Mr. Rose bred Stamboul, one of the
greatest trotting stallions that ever
lived. He also bred Alcazar, The Moore
and other famous trotters. Mr. Rose
turned his attention to running horses
and had a string which was known all
over the United States. He was well
known at Saratoga, Long Branch and
other racetracks.
Mr. Rose spent his money like , a
prince and was known as a man who
would not "hedge" on any proposition.
He won and lost thousands of dollars
on the racetrack, but no one ever heard
him complain when fortune turned
against him. Mr. Rose sold the Sunny
Slope ranch to an English syndicate
about ten years ago. It Is said that the
property brought $1,000,000.
Soon after he sold the Sunny Slope
ranch Mr. Rose built a home at the
corner of Fourth street and Grand ave
nue. The house was then the most
costly and beautiful in Los Angeles.
Mr. Rose spared no expense to make
his home luxurious and he Imported
fine woods for the floors and staircases.
He dressed the walls with rare paint
ings and bric-a-brac, and poured out
his money to make his home comfort
able and beautiful. ; ,;
Mr. Rose leaves a widow and eight
children. Five married daughters and
three sons reside in this city. The
daughters are Mrs. Nina Wachtel, Mrs.
Annie Sanderson, Mrs. J. W. Montgom
ery, Mrs. George Easton and Mrs. Al
bert Pilse. The sons are Harry Rose,
L. J. Rose Jr. and Guy Rose.
No arrangements for the funeral have
yet been considered.
Leonard John Rose was born in Ba
varia, Germany, in 1827. His parents
immigrated to the United States when
he was 12 years of age. The family re
sided at Waterloo, 111., while Mr. Rose
attended - Shurtliff College in Alton.
After leaving school he settled at
Qulncy, where he engaged In the dry
goods business for a number of. years.
Then he removed to Van Buren County,
lowa, .where he continued in the same
line of business. â¢'â â ;-
Mr. Rose started for California, and
after many hardships he finally reached
and settled in the old city of Santa Fe,
N. M., where he engaged in the hotel
business. During the two years . that
he managed the hotel it was financially
successful and - when Mr. Rose and his
family removed to Los Angeles he was
comparatively, a wealthy man.-: He
bought the Sunny slope' ranch Jof 2000
acres and In; a few years increased its
annual output of wine from a few hun
dred gallons to 750.000 gallons of differ
ent kinds of wine and 125,000 gallons of
brandy. 'â , '-â â : \ â  â â /â . â  "-â¢â¢
Mr. Rose, first served the -public as
State Senator from Los Angeles County
for the term commencing ; inIBBL Â__

MANILA, May 17.â Although the
rebels still threaten San Fer
nando in considerable force.
large numbers of natives, a ma
jority of them having families with
their household goods, are moving daily
to the towns inside the American lines,
at Apalit. especially.
Many of the richer Filipinos are com
ing to Manila and laborers are resum
ing work in the rice fields. The latter
show their respect' for American sover
eignty by removing their hats to the
passing trains carrying troops.
Owing to the bad condition of the
wagon roads the work of repairing the
railroad is being actively pushed. All
the broken bridges haver been .trestled
and only a few excavations, remain to
be filled up. Trains will undoubtedly be
running through to San Fernando in a
few days. The only natives there are
some Filipinos from Macabebe who are
trading with our soldiers. ,
At daylight to-day Lieutenant Hill.
who, with twenty-five men .of the
Fourth Infantry, was concealed. in the
trenches near Pasig. was attacked by a
force of rebels who evidently Imagined
they could capture one of our outposts
because only a few shots had been fired
by the American force.' A few volleys
put the enemy to flight,* the, rebels los
ing five men killed and a number of
wounded.
The army gunboat Napidan has re
turned here from the lake, having been
disabled by a cannon shot from a rebel
position near Santa Cruz, which broke
her rudder post.
MANILA, May 18.â Major Kobbes
column reached San Luis, eight miles
up the Rio Grande, yesterday, meeting
With but slight opposition. One man
was wounded during a brush with the
rebels. '!:y"'l'i'2~':-- ,
At daybreak to-day the American
began a further advance toward Can
da ha. about six miles up the river be
yond San Luis.
The insurgents along General Mac-
Arthur's front evacuated their posi
tions during the night time, presumably
moving further up the railroad.
The Twenty-third Infantry has sailed
on the transport Leon XIII for Jolo Isl
and to relieve the Spanish garrison
there.
Agulnaldo and his Cabinet are report
ed to have met at San Isidro before the
Americans arrived and captured that
place and decided to send a commission
to Otis Ito accept the terms of the
proclamation. General Lawton was un
aware of this decision when he ; ad
vanced upon the city. The Filipino
leaders then retired, but a commission
bearing Aguinaldo's submission is ex
pected to-morrow. -(
LAWTON DRIVING THE
ENEMY TO THE MOUNTAINS
WASHINGTON, May 17.â That the
Insurgents are disintegrated and de
moralized is perfectly manifest from
the press dispatches and the cable news
from General Otis received ' to-day.
GeneralLawton, who was pushing I the
line of the rebel retreat along the Rio
Grande, has flung his advance, which at
last advices was resting at San Miguel,
northward about . twelve - miles, has
taken San Isidro, the second insurgent
capital, and when .General Otis' dis- |
patch was sent was still pressing the
enemy northward. The fact that he is |
sustaining few losses in his forward -
movement, although in almost con- j
tinual contact with the enemy, is an
other proof of their utter demoraliza- '
tion. According to General Otis' cable
they continue to throw up ihtrench
ments, but General Lawton's strategy j
outflanks each position in turn, keep- j
ing them in full retreat and giving |
them no opportunity to reorganize
their scattered forces. It will, soon be ;
the mountains or the sea for the in
surgents. As our troops could be trans
ported by sea to the mouth of the Agn.o '
and a new base of operations estab
lished there it would be folly for them
to take that course. Scattered,' demor- j
alized and disheartened it Is almost
certain that the rebels in desperation
will retreat Into the fastnesses of the i
mountains, where they, would be safe |
from. pursuit . and where they could
keep up, a guerrilla warfare indefinitely ;
until their leaders came to their senses. '
Over fifty-five miles, as the crow files, j
the rebels have been forced back.
The situation of tne insurgents is ;
desperate. Mac-Arthur's division drove !
them back along the line of the railroad
toward Dagupan, on the bay of Lin
gayen, as. far as Calumpit. All efforts
to impede his progress by the destruc
tion of bridges was frustrated. ' The in- j
surgents were forced out of their
strongest positions. Simultaneously i
General Lawton. moved to the right !
in a wide detour toward Norzaga Bay,
the eastern limit of open country, with I
the intention of turning the enemy's '
position- and crushing him between the
two columns. Although unsuccessful j
in this, the enemy, to- make good his j
escape, was compelled to abandon his !
line of retreat along the railroad and !
retire up the Rio Grande. J The insur- j
gents by this move displayed consider- i
able adroitness in the art of war, as it j
made it incumbent upon the Americans
to get their supplies to the front along
a new line. v ;.-â â¢.
This had been comparatively easy
along the line of the railroad, which
had been repaired as the troops ad
vanced. The change in the line of re
treat of the rebels stopped Lawton's
advance for several days, It is pre
sumed; as no I movement until to-day
bad been reported since Friday. Mean
time, it is presumed, supplies were sent
forward up the Rlov Grande in cascos
(native barges), under the escort of the
gunboats commanded by Major Kobbes.
The latter was reported to have 1500
men with him designed for the rein
forcement of Lawton. The supplies
probably having arrived at a point op
posite Lawton, whose column had ad
vanced along a line several miles east
and parallel with the river, were deliv
ered to him during the push to San
Isidro. This cleared the province of
Bulacan. v ?V'
East of Norzaga Bay is a wall of
mountains extending to the sea. These
mountains run directly north on the
left flank of the rebel retreat along the
valley of the Rio Grande" to about ten
miles north of San Isidro, where they
trend westward and form the source
of that river. 5 It is into the foothills
of these mountains that Lawton is now
driving * the scattered . and demoralized
force of the. Insurgents. Lawton has
with him the Third and Twenty-second
Infantry, one battalion of the Seven
teenth, the Second ' Oregon, First North
Dakota/Thirteenth Minnesota and a
squadron of the Fourth Cavalry.. No
information has yet been received as
to the Twelfth- Infantry and Light Bat
tery E- of the First Artillery, which

Marin's Sheriff Swears in Depu
ties to Prevent the Lynching
of Oppenheimer.
â Â» â
Special Dispatch to The Call. "
Special Dispatch to The Call. " >
Tuesday's tragedy at San Quen
tin Prison is, it woula seem from
the indications, but the forerunner
of a series. Desperate convicts
in the prison, in a state of almost
open revolt, are threatening the
lives of guards and prison offi
cials, and no steps have been taken
to restrain them.
On the other hand, the stabbing
of Guard McDonald by Oppen
heimer has so stirred public feel
ing in Marin County that the citi
zens have already organized a vig
ilance committee to lynch the con
vict when he is taken to San Ra
fael for trial.
SAN QUENTIN PRISON, May 17.-v
When Guard James McDonald
was assaulted with a knife and
stabbed seven times by Convict
Jacob Oppenheimer it was prophe
sied by those familiar with the circum
stances that only the most drastic
measures would prevent others losing
their lives. This prophecy was seen to
be true to-day, the indications of a re
volt being so plain that even a blind
man might realize that a crisis waa
near at hand. Five men are in con
stant danger, among them being Dr.
Lawlor, the prison physician.
Guard McDonald's condition Is such
to-night that it is deemed a question of
only a few hours when his life will be
at an end. The knowledge of this fact
has so infuriated the rest of the guards
and the friends of McDonald in San
Rafael that it is a serious question
whether Oppenheimer will ever be tried
for the murder. A number of men are
already banded together for the pur
pose of lynching the murderer when he
is taken to San Rafael for trial, and
Sheriff Taylor, who became acquainted
with the condition of affairs this even
ing, has sworn in twenty-five men in
order to be in a position to cope with
the would-be lynchers when the time
arrives for the Marin County authori
ties to take the case of the desperate
criminal in hand.
It is now known that the murderous
assault on McDonald was planned two
weeks ago, the chief conspirators being
Oppenheimer, "Jack" O'Neill. a
life-timer, and John Phillips, alias
"Frisco Slim." Oppenheimer and
"Frisco . Slim" were in the dun
geon together and both were re
leased at the same time, about ten days
ago. While incarcerated in the dungeon

when the word went through the jute

ler," was the sentence that passed from
breaker to breaker, and many of the

Moller it was McDonald who had been
stabbed.
The convicts in the jute mill were so
surly to-day and obeyed orders so
grudgingly that the slim force' of
guards became alarmed, and after v
consultation decided the condition wa*
so alarming that unless something were
done it would be really a suicidal pro
position for them to remain on duty.
Both.O'Nelll and "Frisco Slim" were so
open in their threats that several of
the "trusties" became cognizant of the
danger ahead for the guards and ac
quainted them with their perilous posi
tion.

lor. Only two months ago "FriscO
Slim" made a savage assault on him id
the hospital department, and but ft*
the Interference of another convict
murder might have been committed at
that time. Dr. Lawlor was lucky

gerous position and his assailant was
incarcerated In the dungeon. In view
of his intractable nature and the trou
ble previously given by Phillips, the
Prison Directors ordered him placed in
the dungeon. He was kept there until
ten days ago, when he was released by

gar. As soon as Dr. Lawlor was ap
prised of the release he went to Captain
Edgar and told that official the man
was desperate and had threatened to
kill him (Lawlor) on the first oppor
tunity, and he should be kept In the
dungeon until he became humbled.

WILLOWS MUSICIAN
WILL TOUR EUROPE
WILLOWS, May 17â The peo
ple of Glenn County are great
ly pleased over the fact that
the talented musician. Miss
Mamie Small Randolph, has
been called to Paris by a cablegram
from her former teacher. Professor
Joseph Sancho, who has been engaged
to give a season of concerts at the
French capital. Miss Randolph is the
daughter Of Dr. and Mrs. J. A. Ran
dolph of Willows. She is a decided
blonde, of slender, willowy and graceful
form, and pretty of features. Her age
is 22. She has a wonderful talent for
music, having studied since early child
hood. Three years ago she graduated
first In her class from Beethoven Con
servatory of Music, St. Louis, Mo., and
soon thereafter took a year's course of
lessons on the mandolin and bandurria
of Professor Sancho in San Francisco.
Miss Randolph plays the piano, the
guitar, the bandurria and the mandolin
with equal excellence of execution. She
is partial to high-grade and classic
music, and will take part in the* series
of septet concerts to be given by Pro
fessor Sancho and class. Miss Ran
dolph will be the traveling companion
of Mrs. Joseph Sancho on the tour of
will be all serene m no time."
Dr. Lawlor said he would do nothing
of the kind; that if the man would
come to him of his own accord and
promise to make no more attempts on
his life he would have nothing more
to say. That was the end of the mat-
ter until the assault on McDonald by
â¢ 'ppenheimer, when Phillips renewed
his threats and said he would "do up
not only that old 'cuss' Lawlor. but
Henry Moller as well." Then "Jack"
O'Neill came out with a series of
threats, among them being a statement
that on the first opportunity he in-
tended to i kill Guard Yoho and Guard
Randolph. Randolph is Captain Ed-
gar's assistant and Yoho is the man
who arrived on the scene of the as-
sault yesterday in time to knock Op-
penheimer senseless with a cane and
thereby incur the enmity of all those
who belong to the "gang."
At 5 o'clock this evening Guards
Yoho, Holier, Leahy and Howard Mor-
row, whose life is endangered because
he happened to be on intimate terms
with McDonald, went In a body to Cap-
tain Edgar and petitioned him to place
O'Neill and Phillips in the dungeon.
These two were represented as the ring-
leaders in the plan to dispose of Dr.
Lawlor and the four guards, and the
-aptain was told that if they were not
placed where they could have no means
of counseling and encouraging the
other convicts it was only a question
of a short time when human blood.
and the blood of freemen at that, would
gÃgain stain the floor of the jute mill.
Captain Edgar is asserted to have said
that while there might be some dan-
ger It was his opinion thae*the convicts.
knowing the heated feeling existing
among the guards and officials on ac-
count of the stabbing of McDonald.
would not attempt another murder at
this time. He, therefore, refused to in-
carcerate the ringleaders, and said the
matter should be called to the atten
tion of the Prison Directors at their
next meeting. The men are exasperat-
ed at this reply, and do not hesitate
to say that if more blood is not spilled
before the next meeting of the direc-
tors it will be lucky for the marked
-lien.
McDonald's condition to-night Is re-
tarded as hopeless. He was visited by
Dr. Lawlor at 9 o'clock, and the follow-
ing statement was made by the physi
cian on leaving the office of Captain
Birlem:
"The patient has developed traumatic
pneumonia, which generally follows a
severe wound by a knife or a gunshot,
Â»nd is spitting blood. His legs have
:urned purple from the knee down and
heat has to be kept applied to them.
This discoloration is due to a sluggish
circulation, and the low condition of
McDonald may be gauged from the fact
that the discoloration is seen even at
:he neck, in the form of spots as large
is a silver dollar. The temperature is
101.4, the pulse 112 and very erratic and
:he respiration 44. It is my belief that
:he man cannot last forty-eight hours."

POLITICAL OPPOSITION
TO EXPANSION DOCTRINE
NEW YORK, May 17.â special to the!
Sun from Washington says: At a meet- j
Ing of Irish-American Republicans, held j
la the parlors of the Ebbitt House on !
Monday and Tuesday of this week, an
organization was effected which has for
its purpose energetic and organized polit- '
ical opposition to the doctrine of expan- j
slot) and retention of the Philippine Isl- !
ands by the United States.
This movement appears to have had its
origin In New York City some weeks ago,
but has been kept quiet. It was started
by several representative Irish-Ameri
cans including Colonel Patrick O'Ferrall
of this city, who is a pension and patent
attorney of wide acquaintance through
Â°H.t, Lh(L Unitt-d States. At the meeting
which has just closed a society was or
ganized and by-laws and constitution
were, adopted. Beyond the adoption of;
these rules and the appointment of a
committee to issue propaganda and con
duct an extensive correspondence, noth- I
ing was done.
The task of drawing Irish-American
citizens into the party will devolve
largely upon Colonel O'Ferrall, who is
one of the most enthusiastic members of
the organization. Colonel O'Ferrall has I
always been a Republican, and before the â¢
existence of that party was an Abolition- 1
ist. During the last Presidential cam-'
paign he was on the stump six or seven
weeks in the Interest of McKinley. Dis
cussing* the proposed organization Col- '
onel O'Ferrall to-night said; j
"I have always been a Republican, but
I am unalterably opposed to this new doc
trine of imperialism. I believe we should
confine ourselves to this continent and the
islands contiguous. We only bring new :
troubles upon ourselves by going to
Asia and endeavoring to do things which
Spain has tried to do for 300 years and
failed. So far as I am concerned, loyal â¢
publican as I have always been, I shall j
vote for the ticket of that party which
pledges itself against Imperialism and
these new doctrines that threaten to
wreck the country. We expect to have
many additions to our ranks, although
we shall do little beyond conducting cor
respondence until we see what the Re
publican party intends to do. If it is the I
policy of that party to keep the Philip- \
pines and fasten upon us an imperialism :
Inconsistent with Republican institutions,
then the mass of the Irish- Americans will I
vote against that party."

PREPARING FOR THE
DISABLED VETERANS
WASHINGTON. May 17.-In anticipa
tion of the return of many disabled sol
diers from the Philippines, : Cuba and
Porto Rico, preparations are being made
by the authorities of the Soldiers' Home
here to largely increase the facilities and
capacity of that institution. Last year
previous to the beginning of the actual'
campaign service in the tropics,, the in
mates of the home numbered in the neigh
borhood of 750 men. Now the number has
increased to 820. None of the regulars
who have been engaged in service In the
Philipp nes have yet arrived at the home
but it Is probable that the number will
exceed that from the combined Cuban
and Porto Rican service, in view of the
?/Lc.ater. ,en&th Â»f "me during which the
Philippine forces have been actively en
gaged. The changes and improvements
which are about to be made have been
under contemplation for several months
past. According to estimates made the
entire work will cost about $100,000.

POPULISTS WHO
WILL NOT BE IN
LINE FOR BRYAN
Plans of the Middle=of-the=Road
Men for Defeating AH At-
tempts at Fusion,
KANSAS CITY, May 17.â address to
the Populists' of the country was issued
here to-night by the national organiza
tion of the People's party with the In
dorsement of the National Reform Press
Association. These two bodies, whose
common object" is to keep . the Populist
party in the middle of the road, avoiding
fusion with other political parties every
where, had been In- session here since
Tuesday morning, and the adoption of the
address concluded * their â  labors. *
All was not harmony among these
middle-of-the-road leaders, and only
after long, and heated discussions was an
address agreed upon. A hard fight was
made to secure the indorsement of Whar
ton Barker and Ignatius Donnelly for
President and Vice President in 1900, but
this the committee refused to recom
mend. Instead a thorough organization
of the middle-of-the-roaders in every sec
tion of the. country is urged, with the
understanding that hey shall enter the
next national campaign with the present
Populist organization, but that wherever
fusion shall gain the day. whether in
county, State or nation, the middle-of
the-roaders shall bolt and begin an active
campaign to carry, out their principles.
The alleged fusionist tendencies of
Marion Butler, chairman of the Populist
National Committee, are provided against
in a clause which authorizes the national
organization to call a national conven
tion if it shall be convinced that the Na
tional Chairman or National Committee
are playing Into the hands of the fusion
lsts.
The address of the committee in sub
stance follows: y>v .:.; v:j.:- -
That the voters of the nation may feel as
sured that the People's party shall not again
be betrayed in national convention or Its work
ing forces passed into the hands of the enemy
and to inspire confidence among the masses in
the integrity of our acts and sincerity of our
demands for independent action as a party, we
respectfully submit to the Populists of the
nation the following plan of action:
Firstâ That the national organization com
mittee hereby Instruct its chairman to pro
ceed with the formation of People's party pre
cinct clubs in all the States, on the plan rec2
ommended by the Cincinnati convention of
September, IS9S, or some relative plan, and to
appoint in each State not having members al
ready selected three members of the national
organization committee and with the assist
ance and advice of these committeemen to se
lect a State organization committee of the
same number of members as the then existing
State committee, and through these commit
teemen to organize as far as possible organi
zation committees In Congressional districts.
counties and voting precincts. Wherever It is
positively known that those members of na
tional. State and other committees now exist
ing are unqualifiedly opposed to fusion with
either of the old parties and for independent,
straightforward action by the People's party
they are to be selected as members of the sev
eral organization committees. w.::i
Secondâ lt shall be the duty of these several
committees to use all honorable means to se
cure the selection of delegates to the various
conventions leading up to the nominating
Presidential convention of 1300, who are op
posed to fusion; and failing In this, to provide'
for and send contesting delegates to the sev
eral conventions. That Is to say. If those who
are opposed to fusion are unfairly or dishonor
ably treated in the county convention, they i

QUEEN VICTORIA
NOT LOSING SIGHT

founded.
â â
Special Cable to The Call and the New York
Herald. Copyrighted, 1839, by James Gor-
don Bennett. _
LONDON, May 17.â further denial of
the unfounded story to the effect that
Queen Victoria had lost the sight of her
left eye and that her right eye was nearly
obscured by a cataract were necessary it
was to be found in her Majesty's cheerful
appearance and keen enjoyment at the i
sight of the great crowd which lined the
way between Buckingham Palace and
the Victoria and Albert Museum to-day. j
The Queen rode in an open carriage and
was not protected from the sun by. a
parasol,- and did not wear any glasses,
colored or otherwise, until the ceremonies
of laying of the cornerstone began.
I have been informed on the best au
thority that the Queen's eyesight Is as
good as could be expected in a woman |
of her age, and it has not been impaired j
during her visit to Cimlez.
A. correspondent of a London evening pa
per who made Inquiries at Windsor of one '
of her physicians in residence there was
distinctly told that there was no truth In j
the report that the Queen's eyes were se
riously affected.
Queen Victoria laid the foundation
stone of the Victoria and Albert Mu
seum. She drove through the streets,
lined with troops from Buckingham Pal
ace, the royal carriage being escorted by
a detachment of the Life Guards. Her
Majesty was accompanied by several
Princes and Princesses, and other royal
personages met her at the museum, i
where, a raised dais was reserved for j
them. The embassadors, members of the i
Cabinet and leading state officials were at
the pavilion. An enormous number of
people had gathered along the route when
the Queen started from the palace at 4:15
p.' m. The houses were decorated and
there were strings of flags across tho
roads. The sun shone brilliantly.
A gayly dressed assemblage witnessed
the. ceremony, which the Queen per
formed without leaving her carriage and
amid much enthusiasm.
The ceremonies resembled a miniature
jubilee. American flags were'conspicu
ous among the decorations. Inside the
museum tbe scene recalled the spectacle
at St. Paul's Cathedral upon the occasion
of the Jubilee. . ! - ::.;-â¢.'>
The Marquis of Salisbury, Lord Rose
bery, all the Cabinet Ministers and the
diplomatic corps were present, nearly all
of them wearing brilliant uniforms. The
arrival of the Ministers in splendid equlp
pages, with footmen, was very pictur- j
esque. United States Embassador Joseph
H. Choate and Mrs. Choate drove up in
a plain carriage, Mr. Choate wearing a
dress suit, In remarkable contrast to the
uniforms of the other diplomats. Mr.
and Mrs. Choate were given a prominent
place on the platform, and while await
ing the arrival of the Queen, the Premier,
the First Lord of .the Admiralty, "George
J. Goschen, and others chatted with
them. ' - -'
Her Majesty looked remarkably well.
She: was dressed in black except for a
white plume in her bonnet. v She took the
greatest Interest In the mechanical part
of the stone laying, talking at length with
the Prince of Wales on the subject. The
Queen. was seated in a four-horse open
carriage- with Scotchmen on the rumble,
and escorted by outriders.
The : national- anthem was sung ,by
pupils of the Royal College of Music and
afterward a madrigal, -specially â¢ com
posed by the poet laureate, Alfred Austin, I
was sung to music written by Sir Alex
ander Mackenzie, principal of the Royal
Academy. The Archbishop of Canter
bury, Primate of all England, read ; the
prayers.

NEGRO ASSASSIN
RESISTS ARREST
Holds a Posse of Police
at Bay.
â â¢ â
Special Dispatch to The Call.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WASHINGTON, May 17.â Humphrey
Taylor, a negro suspected of the Rosen
stein murder at Slidell, Md., to-day
shot and killed Police Sergeant Fritz
Passau, wounded Policeman Gow and
kept a posse of half a dozen officers at
bay from the loft of a house for nearly
two hours. Dozens of shots were ex
changed between the officers and the
fugitive, who surrendered only when
preparations were made to burn, the
premises.
Last Saturday morning Louis Rosen
stein and .wife, who kept a
small store at Slidell, were found
insensible, and horribly wounded
in their storeroom. Rosenstein
soon died from his injuries and
the woman is believed to be near death.
Suspicion fell upon . the negro, Hum
phrey Taylor, alias Brown, who had
disappeared. The police to-day located
him in a house near the scene of the
murder. â ' V-; :,'-~:.: : -'
When they entered Taylor opened fire
from the trapdoor In which he had
taken refuge. Sergeant Passau received
tÂ«vc bullets through his chest and Gow
was wounded in the hand. Taylor re
treated to a corner of the loft and the
reserve of the two precincts was
called out to surround the house. Mean
time the shooting had attracted several
thousand persons. The negro was not
dislodged until preparations to burn
the place had been begun.
Surrounded by officers with drawn re
volvers, he was hustled out of the house
to the patrol wagon. The crowd surged
forward with shouts of "Lynch him!
Burn him!" and made a rush for the
prisoner. A rope, was' obtained and the
mob made a desperate effort to place it
around the wretch's neck. The cool
ness of ' the officers ! saved Taylor,
though he was "rather badly disfigured
by blows from the nearest of the crowd.
The feeling against the prisoner con
tinues very strong, and during the
evening over a thousand persons gath
ered at the station where the man is
confined. There were many threats ut
tered and .suggestions made that the
man should be lynched. Finally the
police department increased the force
around the station by the addition of
about thirty officers and succeeded in
restoring quiet.
DATE FOR THE BIG FIGHT.
Fitzsimmons and Jeffries -Will Argue
y>.:>f Their Case on June 9. \::{-'*
' NEW YORK, May 17.â The day of the
Bob Fltzsimmons-Jeffries fight before
the Coney, Island Athletic Club has
been fixed as June . 9, j and "George Siler
of 'Chicago will be referee. The selec
tion of Decoration day as the day for
the big fight aroused so much opposi
tion among sporting men that the man
agers of the club and the managers of
the fighters j rrfet to-day. in William
Brady's office to I discuss the situation.
It was argued that Decoration day is
usually a big sporting day, when there
are many, events of interest scheduled.
and that to have the fight on that day
would ' mean a smaller attendance and
smaller gate receipts.
It was decided finally to postpone the
fight until June 9. The arrangement
was satisfactory to all concerned.

DEADLY PRANKS
OF THE CYCLONE
Loss of Life and Prop-
erty in lowa.
â Â« â
Special Dispatch to The Call.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
MANCHESTER, lowa, May 17.â The de
tails of the cyclone which passed over
the northern part of this county last
night are harrowing. As a result of the
storm four persons are dead and i.hr:e
others are fatally injured. The dead:
WALTER SHEPPARD AND HIS SON,
13 years old.
J. B. JACOBS.
GEORGE LAND.
The fatally Injured: Mrs. Walter Shep
pard and two sons, 3 and 6 years old.
The seriously Injured: George Luaricey,
Tony Sheppard, 10 years old; Tony Fritz,
Mrs. A. Bigelow, Ed Krumple and wife,
Mrs. William McKlnney. j_manuel Ride
nour and v. *c, Miss Ridenour, C. -liersen.
Every farm house In the track of the
storm cloud for a width of forty rods is
either totally destroyed or wrecked. At
Blgelow's the family took shelter In the
cellar. A 'young babe was torn from the
mother's arm and carried twenty -rive
rods without injury. At the Klaus Ceme
tery only one monument Is left standing.
and some of the tombstones were found
two miles away. The Klaus school house
was blown to pieces and the Methodist
Church is a wreck.
The Ridenour family took refuge In the
cellar. Three are injured. Mr. Ridenour
was lifted bodily out of the cellar by the
force of the wind. The Sheppard family
were in the house, which was smashed to
kindling. Two of the sons were carried
400 yards and one was killed outright.
The father died this afternoon. The other
children will die. C. Mlersen, J. B. Jacobs
and George Lang were returning from
Elkport and were caught directly in the
path of the storm. Jacobs and Lang
jumped out of the wagon and were car
ried up Into the air. Their bodies came to
the ground mangled almost beyond recog
nition. Mlersen whipped his horses out of
the wind. Jumped from the wagon and es
caped with slight injury. The team turned
and ran. into the storm again, and both
horses were killed. i ' .:â ' ."â¢
The storm exhausted Itself a mile north
of Colesburg. The damage to -property
Is beyond estimation.
NO TRACE OF JOOST.
Search for Missing Sonoma Farmer
Proves Unavailing.
SONOMA, May 17.â Joost. who has
been missing since last Saturday morn
ing, has- not yet been found, although
diligent search has been made over a big
scope of country. A reward of $25 has
been offered for the recovery of his body
by Mrs. Joost, who is nearly distracted
over the sudden disappearance.
Mr. Joost left home about 9 o'clock Sat
urday, In company with his son Carl, who
is about 10 years old. He went to catch
some horses which were running loose in
his place. Having secured one he sent
the boy In with it and told him he would
await his return. The boy returned as
instructed, but his father had disap
peared. - â¢ â¢.;-â¢ â "-'- '- < V .'-â 
Joost had been in ill health for some
time and was very weak. It is supposed
that while searching for another horse he
wandered off In the thick underbrush and
perished. _

GIFT TO MRS. STANFORD.
Valuable Collection of Chinese Coins
of Ancient Date.
STANFORD UNIVERSITY, May 17.â
Mrs. Stanford has been made the recip
ient of a valuable collection of Chinese
coins. They were presented by Charles
Seymour, former United States Consul to
China. While the collection is not a large
one, containing only 330 coins, it
is exceedingly valuable, as some of the
pieces date back as far as Noah and the
flood. Almost every dynasty from the
fire-breathing Shinn, who reigned B. C.
2215, to the present Emperor is repre
sented. The gift will be placed in the
museum and will add greatly to the al
ready numerous collections of ancient
coins. _ . v
Two Judgments Given.
SAN JOSE, May Two judgments
were given against the Santa Clara Val
ley Agricultural Association - to-day by
Judge Lorlgan. W. C. Andrews obtained
one for $7*12 and A. B. McNeil another
for $940. The society has been in debt for
a number of years, and its race grounds
near the city are mortgaged for about
$12,000. A plan Is now being considered
for clearing this debt by selling part of
the tract for building lots.

CONSULAR AGENT STONED.
Investigation of a Mob's Assault
Upon Uncle Sam's Representative.
WASHINGTON, May Secretary Hay
has directed Minister Bryan at Rio de
Janeiro to make an investigation of the
report that the United States Consular
officer at Manaos, Brazil, was stoned by a
mob.
These instructions follow the receipt of
' newspaper clippings sent by the Minister
! telling of the outrage, but up to the time
' his letter was sent- he had received no
'. confirmatory' advices from John C. Red
! man. United States Consular Agent at
Manaos. It was stated to-day that the
i department had received a cablegram
i announcing that the report was correct,
I but Secretary Hay could not be seen to
verify it. The attention of the Navy De
! partment has been called to the matter.
| If It has been found that the consulate
was assaulted by a mob, the Brazilian
Government will be called upon to make
the usual amends. The authorities treat
the matter lightly, in view of the well-
known friendship of Brazil for this coun-
try.

Special Dispatch to The Call.
Call Headquarters, Wellington Hotel,
Washington, May 17.
Representative De Tries, the only
Democrat re-elected to Congress from
California last fall, was in Washington
to-day. Since Congress adjourned he
has been both South and East as well
as on the Pacific Coast. He says that
the Governor of California is going to
appc-int Burns to the vacancy in the
Senate left by the failure of the Legis
lature to elect, and that information
comes from the best source that both
Quay and Burns are to be seated. He i
says that if the plan is carried out pub
lic sentiment will, in his judgment, i
change the complexion of the next Cal
ifornia Legislature, and that after the
meeting o-f the new Legislature Burns |
will be succeeded by a Democrat.
California Democrats, he says, are ;
satisfied with Bryan and expect his re
nomination, with a reaffirmation of the
Chicago platform. He acknowledges, '
however, that there is a very hard fight
centering in New York and extending i
into the South and to some extent into
the middle West against Bryan. The j
plan c-f th( opponents of free silver, he
says, Is not to try to prevent the re- j
affirmation of the money plank in the j
platform, but to give greater promi- '
nence to anti-trust and anti-imperial
ism as issues, ignoring the money ques
tion as far as possible.
De Vries started for California to- '
nis-ht.

INDIAN SCHOOL BUILDINGS
RAZED BY A CONFLAGRATION
WADSWORTH, Nev.. May 17.â
An Indian courier came in from
the reservation at Pyramid
Lake to-day, bringing news of
the burning of the Indian school
buildings at that place at 7
o'clock this morning. This fire entails a
severe loss to the Piute children and a
loss of $24,000 to the Government.
The buildings burned included the
two-story frame, known as the "boys'
dormitory, which was about 40x60
feet and connected with the main build
ing on the north. The latter -was a one
story building, also of wood, and cov
ered an area of about 40x100 feet. In
these buildings were the class rooms and
the living and work rooms of the half
dozen teachers and seventy-five Indian
scholars.
The fire originated, it Is supposed, from
a defective flue in the dormitory build
ing. Though i he reservation has a regu
lar fire brigade composed of Indians,
which is drilled regularly for such an
emergency, and which has fairly good ap
pliances for flghtin lire, the flames
gained such headway before the brigade
could get to. work that it was unable to
do anything more than save the resi
de;,,..-, of Major Spriggs, the Indian
Agent: Dr. Harrison, the reservation phy
sician; Mr. Jones, assistant to the agent,
and the chapel, bath house and other
buildings adjoining those destroyed.
The fire drill, through which the schol
ars have been put at regular intervals,
became a serious reality this morning
and probably was the means of saving
a number of lives. Though the hour was
early, most of the regular occupants of
the building were up, but very few of
them were outside of the building when
the alarm was sounded. The children
were promptly formed in line and hur
ried from the building, and there was no
time to spare. Professor D. R. Osborne,
the principal of the school, was in com
mand, and was assisted by his wife. Miss
Olive Waite and J. H. Barr, teachers, and

PACIFIC CABLE
SURVEY ORDERED
British Ship E&eria to

Special Dispatch to The Call.
VANCOUVER, B. C, May 17.â
British survey ship Egeria arrived to-
British survey ship Egeria arrived to
day with specific orders to start at once
day with specific orders to start at once
surveying the route for the projected
surveying the route for the projected
marine cable from Vancouver to Aus
marine cable from Vancouver to Aus
tralia. This order arrived from the
tralia. This order arrived from the
Admiralty by cable since the advices of
Admiralty by cable since the advices of
the loth inst., instructing Commander
the 10th inst., instructing Commander
Smyth of the Egeria to hold his ship
Smyth of the Egeria to hold his ship
in readiness for this trip. The British
Government has evidently changed its
attitude in regard to the cable since
the announcement was made that this
province would contribute one-tenth of
the cost of construction, amounting in
round figures to $1,000,000.
British Columbia is rejoicing to-night
and it is regarded as settled that the
cable will be constructed at an early
date. The Egeria will leave to-morrow
for the British naval station at Es
quimau, where she will provision, and
start for Fanning Island from Van
couver on Monday next.
The Egeria is equipped with the most
modern scientific instruments and for
forty years has been engaged charting
the waters wherever British ships are
wont to navigate. In 1863 she mapped
the Pacific Coast waters for mariners
so effectively that not an alteration lias
been made since. A better vessel could
not have been engaged to find a path
way for one of the most gigantic ma
rine cables ever laid. She is manned
by 100 men and has fifteen guns. Her
officers have been engaged in survey
work for the past twenty-five years.
ACCUSES HER FORMER
HUSBAND OF ARSON
LOS ANGELES, May 17.â 1n a deposi
tion filed to-day in the office of the Coun
ty Clerk, Mrs. Mary Lord Geiss, the di
vorced wife of Isaac Wilson Lord, the
founder of Lordsburg and formerly a San
Bernardino County Supervisor, and one
of the best known, men in Southern Cali
fornia, swears that her former husband
Is an. ex-convict. . Mrs. Qeiss asserts that
Lord was sent to San Quentin in 1856 or
ISSB under the name of George Wilson.
The deponent says Lord . was sent to
prison for grand larceny.
Mrs. Geiss. says that she suspects her
former husband of complicity In having
caused the fire that destroyed the furni
ture store of Dotter & Bradley, now the
Los Angeles Furniture Company, several
years ago. The deposition is to be used
in the suit of George J. Bowron against
Lord,, in which the plaintiff will try to
recover $3000 which it is alleged Lord ob
tained from Bowron by means of false
representations. This suit is the out
growth of the noted "Rebel George" Al
exander case; Â£ Alexander bunkoed Lord
and Bowron out; of $9000 by a gold brick
swindle. . Bowron supplied $3000 of the
sum Bowron now claims that Lord was
in collusion with Alexander, and that the
pair put up the game to rob Bowron.
Mrs. Geiss further deposes that when
Lord spoke about the fire and the stock
that was burned he said it was valued at
$4000, but he thought there was about $22,
--000 insurance. on it.
Mrs. Geiss . is now a resident of Ba
tavia, 111. Her deposition was taken last
Saturday at the office of H. H. Appel,
Bowron's attorney. Mrs. Geiss left for
her Eastern home the next day.
Thomas Lloyd Reed Dead.
SALINAS, May 17.â Thomas Lloyd Reed
died at Castroville this afternoon of can-
cer, after an illness of several months.'
He was a native of Kentucky and 66 years
of age.- He had resided in. Salinas valley
nearly a quarter of a century. A widow
and several children survive him.

RESERVATION SCHOOL BUILDING.
CLANCY ACCUSED
BY SIX JURORS
Inquest Over the Body
of Welch.
of Welch.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
MARTINEZ, May 17.â The inquest over
the body of Henry W. Welch, who was
the body of Henry \V. Welch, who was
murdered at Orlnda, brought out a good
deal of conflicting testimony .and as a re
sult two verdictsâ one charging Clancy,
who did the shooting, with murder, and a
minority verdict acquitting him on the
ground of Justifiable homicide. Joseph
Roland, in whose shop the affray oc
curred, was the only eye-witness. He not
only seemed to have had no liking for the
murdered man, but was apparently ani
mated by a desire* to shield Clancy as
much as possible. The consequence was
that his testimony was as times conflict
ing, especially as to the cause of the
shooting and the manner in which it was
done.
The testimony of the autopsy surgeons,
J. T. Breneman and A. P. Mulligan, was
important and conclusive. The autopsy
revealed the course of the bullets and
showed that the shots were - fired at
Welch while his back was turned to
Clancy. All of the bullets entered from
the back, clearly indicating that Welch
was retreating when killed. .
The majority verdict charging Clancy
with murder Was signed by six of the
eight jurors, the minority finding of jus
tifiable homicide receiving but two signa
tures. ':;.; ::

Marriage Law in Peru.
LIMA. Peru, May 17.â Government
has published a decree in reference : to
non-Catholic marriages. Parties to such
unions must state their religion and de
clare under oath in the presence;' of two
witnesses that they have never been bap
tized as Catholics. Parties contracting a
civil marriage because . of the refusal of
the Roman Catholic â  church to grant ' a
dispensation in case of a difference of re
ligions must present proof vi the refusal.
by Miss Margaret J. Gutelin and Miss E.
K. Robertson, assistants;
The fire occasioned great excitement
among the Indiana at the fandango vil
lage, located only a short distance from
the school. â¢ Many of them had danced
until daylight, but all hands, even down
to the laziest Piute, joined in the effort
to extinguish the flames and save the
other property. Ft is expected the build
ings will be rebuilt, as soon as the neces
sary "r*d tape" has been filed and acted
upon at Washington. Â ..=
The fandango, with all accompaniments,
still runs, despite the fire. Last Satur
day night the Indian police, with Chief
Captain Dave at their head, raided and
closed two or three gambling places
which had been opened by some visit
ing Indians. The visitors protested 'stren
uously, and in a long pow-wow argued
that their games were no more gambling
than "Xyuque" and poker games or bet
ting on the races and pasebali games.
The Piutei would stand no nonsense and
gave the visitors the choice of closing up
or going to jail. They do not allow
Judges of the Groezinger type on the res
ervation, so the places had to close. As
a matter of fact, the games that the
Piutes would not tolerate were percent
age games, with a profit much less than
that of the ordinary racetrack book, or
the games at the Cafe Royal; the Thalia,
Our House, etc.

WALLACE, Idaho, May 17.â Judge
Mayhew this afternoon denied the ap
plication for a writ of habeas corpus
for the two County Commissioners,
Boyle and Stimson, confined at Ward
ner for complicity in the recent rioting.
In rendering his decision he denied
every material contention of the appli
cants. Their main hope was based on
the contention that a suspension of
habeas corpus was a right of the legis
lative branch of the Government rather
than the executive,, unless delegated by
the former in the same manner as
other laws are passed. Mayhew held
this to be erroneous.
Owners or representatives from all
the leading Canyon Creek and Mullan
mines are here to-day. and will stand
solidly by the State officials. One of
them said: "We furnished those men
work at $3 '50 a day when lead was $2 50
and we were barely paying expenses
Now, when we have a chance for a
good profit; they close us down by their
unlawful acts, and we feel that such
men don't deserve work."
Every mine above here is closed or
will be to-night.
JOINT COMMISSION
SOON TO REASSEMBLE
WASHINGTON, May 17.â As the result
of a conference held at the Foreign Of
fice in London between Lord Salisbury,
Sir Julian Pauncefote and Embassador
Choate, the latter acting under instruc
tions from Secretary Hay.it can now be
predicted that the high joint commission
considering .issues between the United
States and Canada will be reassembled
during the summer or early fall. This
outcome Is not yet positively assured
but Mr. Choate's report of the exchanges
at the Foreign Office certainly convey
great encouragement to the officials here
and warrant the expectation that nego
tiations may again be taken up with some
prospect of reaching agreements between
the two branches of the- commission.

PROMPT STEPS TO CHECK THE

â¦
â¦
Three Companies of Infantry and a
n Battery of Artillery Will Pro-
tect the Interests of
Americans.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK. May 17.â The Washing
:on correspondent of the Herald tele-
graphs: To preserve peace and order
In Alaska and to prevent a conflict be
tween the American and Canadian
miners which may injure the good rela
tions existing between the- United
States and Great Britain, orders were
issued by Secretary Alger to-day under
which three companies of infantry will
be sent immediately to that Territory.
These orders have been given with
the approval of the State. Department,
which has forwarded the proper noti
fication to Great Britain to convince
that Government that the United
States is acting in good faith and pro
poses to maintain peace. They are also
due to the failure of Secretary Hay and
Sir Julian Pauncefote to arrange ' a
modus vivendi, which the authorities
say would have prevented the condi
tion of affairs now reported along the
boundary line.
Reports received by the Interior De
partment from Governor Brady show*
that Canadian miners have no regard
for the temporary boundary line which
has been established, and are constant
ly encroaching on American soil.
American miners are very much
wrought up over the matter, and
threats of vengeance, which may any
day be put into execution, are being
uttered. ,:.;-". Â£
General Merritt has been directed to
send one company of infantry and Gen
eral Merriam two companies, and place
them under the orders of Major Gen
eral Shafter, commanding the Depart
ment of California, which includes
Alaska. The instructions given to Gen
eral Shafter require him to establish a
new post at Pyramid harbor, on Chil
kat Inlet, not far from the boundary
line, the point where danger of a con
flict is greatest. It has also been de
termined to replace two companies of
the Fourteenth Infantry, which were
stationed at Dyea and Fort Wrangel.
These troops have been ordered ;to
proceed to the Philippines for duty.
Dyea is not far from Chilcoot Pass and
only a short distance from the boun
dary line. Battery A of the Third Ar
tillery, which is at Circle City, will be
retained .at that point. With three
companies and Battery A the officials
believe peace and order will be pre
served, but the dispatch of troops will
not stop the efforts of the State De
partment to secure a settlement of the
Alaskan boundary dispute, even be
fore the meeting In August of the
Anglo-Canadian American High Joint
Commission, which will certainly dis
cuss the boundary question should it
still exist.
Senator Fairbanks and former Secre
tary of State Foster, members of the
High Joint Commission, were in con
ference to-day with Secretary Hay re
garding the negotiations which Embas
sador Choate has been conducting in
London with reference to the Alaskan
boundary. Senator Fairbanks will pro
ceed to Alaska to examine conditions
there for himself and expects to return
to Washington the latter part of July
in order to be present at the reas
sembling of the commission.
BOLD OPERATIONS OF
A NEW RELIGIOUS SECT
NEW YORK, May 17.â A Bangor (Me.)
special to the Herald says. A strange re
ligious sect, known as the Disciples of the
Holy Ghost, whoso operations in various
parts of Maine within the past year and
more have caused great excitement among
the weak-minded, have obtained a strong
foothold In the town of Levant, a few
miles west of Bangor, where they had
been holding meetings and making con
verts for several months past.
Ruel Clement, a recent convert,- has
turned over his farm and all its Imple
ments and stock, except swine, to the
leaders of the new sect. . The swine, being
regarded as unclean beasts, have been
slaughtered and their carcasses buried.
The . farmer has now j gone to Durham,
there to work in the co-operative colony,
while his fine farm in Levant is being
managed by a disciple of the new faith
named George Higgins. â¢
Nathaniel McKuslck, another farmer,
was looked upon as a likely convert, but
he backslid. Disciples called the other
day and asked him if he was ready to
give his property to God. He replied that
he could notâ that the farm, was God's
already, and he was merely exercising a
stewardship over it.
At Durham, where the sect has its head
quarters, has been erected on a high hill
a queer-looking building, which they call
the Temple of Shiloh, this being the cen
ter and fountain from which is dispensed
the new doctrine of comfort, happiness
and eternal salvation through faith pure
and simple. J: -.'".' tr
PICQUART REPLIES
TO ACCUSATIONS
NEW YORK. May 17.â A cable to the
Sun from Paris says: The Figaro this
morning publishes a memorial which Col
onel Picquart sent yesterday to the Court
of Cassation. He points out that four
heads of the charges under which he is
a prisoner are: Communicating to Le
Blois the information drawn from the
Esterhazy dossier, communicating infor
mation drawn from the Dreyfus dossier,
forgery and using forgery.
Replying to the first accusation, which
Siben fully developed on August 24, 1898,
alleging that Picquart was not attacked
when he communicated to Le Blois in
June, 1897, information from the Ester
hazy dossier, Picquart points out that the
machinations against him began in Au
gust, 1896, the date when it was discov
ered that the bordereau was written by
Esterhazy, and that the secret dossier did
not aoply to Dreyfus when Picquart com
municated his discovery to his superiors.
He gives Incidents of the machinations,
pointing out Colonel Henry's duplicity in
writing friendly letters to him while con
spiring against '.. him. When Henry was
unmasked he retorted with, a menacing
letter, written in complicity with Gen
erals Boisdeffre and Gonz, in which he
threatened to turn Picquart's discoveries
regarding Esterhazy â  against himself.
Picquart, seeing that < strong Influences
were working for his destruction, con
veyed to Le Blois the minimum of infor
mation necessary for his defense.

JUSTIN BROWN ON
TRIAL FOR MURDER
Witness Bennett Describes the Cold-
Blooded Killing of James A.
Cain.
EAKERSFIELD. May 17. â Justin
Brown, who killed James A. Cain near
Brown, who killed James A. Cain near
Woody on January 30, is on trial for
Woody on January 30, is on trial for
murder. Cain sent a boy to Brown's
cabin to borrow a cart. The boy returned
without the vehicle. Cain then went to
see Brown about the cart. They quar
reled and Brown shot and killed Cain.
Brown alleges self-defense.
â¢ The taking of testimony began this
morning.. The main witness was "Jack"
Bennett, who was the only eye-witness.
Bennett testified that he and Brown were
seated in the house, when Cain rode up
and hitched his horse near the house.
Brown arose, went out on the porch, drew
a pistol and shot at Cain. Cain threw up
his- hands and exclaimed:
"My God! Don't shoot me don't shoot
me-!" "/-/â¢-â¢â :V-;'.;.'--.y - â â¢â ' â  - â¢
Brown kept advancing and shooting and
Cain backing till he reached the post
where his horse was tied. He was then
bleeding from wounds. Brown rushed up
close and deliberately fired a bullet into
Cain's body and then kicked and cursed
him. Cain walked a few steps and fell.
Other testimony indicated that Brown
and Mrs. Cain were intimate. Cain had
six children. Brown is a young man.
The case is attracting wide attention.

Two Banks Consolidated.
SAN JOSE. May 17.â consolidation
of the Citizens' Bank and the Bank of
Santa Clara Valley at Santa Clara is
contemplated about July 1. Judge J. R.
Lewis, president of the Citizens' Bank.
will be president of the combined bank
and David Henderson, president of the
Valley Bank, will be manager. The new
banking house will be known as the Val

Sister Augustine O'Neil Dead.
SAN JOSE. May 17.â Sister Augustine
O'Neil died .at the O'Connor Sanitarium
this morning of heart failure and general
exhaustion. She came here three months
ago from San Francisco for her health.
Sister Augustine had been a . teacher In
St. Francis Technical School, San Fran
cisco, for eleven years. She was a native
of Massachusetts and 35 years of age.

Special Dispatch tn The Call.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SANTA CRUZ, May 17.â The session of:
the (rand Lodge, Knights of Pythias, this
afternoon was a busy one. U. H. Morri
son of San Francisco, c. 1,. Pardee of
Areata and F. L. Weber of San Bernar-
dino were apnointed on the committee on
necrology. A cordial invitation was re
ceived from Hermlon Temple of Rathbone I
Sisters to be present at its reception in
the evening at the St. George Hotel. The
committee on redisricting the State made
a number of recommendations. Greetings
were received from the Grand Lodge of |
Missouri, which is in session. To-morrow
morning the election of officers will take I
place.
Salinas was selected as the next meet-
Salinas was selected as the next meet- \
ing place of the Grand Lodge.
This evening at the armory there oc- i
curred an exemplification of the Knight .
rank by the grand officers. This was fol
lowed by a banquet at the Sea Beach
Hotel. The spacious dining-room was
decorated with Pythian colors, yards and
yards of yellow red and blue bunting
having been used. The tables were dec
orated with dainty smilax and choicest
flowers from the garden of the Sea Beach
Hotel. Covers were arranged for 328 per
sons and all the seats were taken. The
menu was an elegant one.
Past Grand Chancellor Carl E. Lindsay
was toastmaster and introduced the
speakers and announced the toasts, which !
were as follows: "The Grand Lodge,"
Grand Chancellor C, L. Pattori ; "The
Pythian Warfare." Supreme Representa- !
tive A. J. Buckles of Fairfield; "The
Eschscholtzia and the Lily," Past Grand
Chancellor George Samuels of Oakland;
"Our Country, Every Pythian's Pride,"
Past Grand Chancellor George E. Church j
of Fresno: "Pythians in the Southland,"
Grand Vice Cham H. R. Arndt of
San Diego; "The Pythian Flag." General j
C. F. McGlashan of Truckee; "The Ladies,
God Bless Them," Past Grand Chancellor
John H. Gyselaar of Eureka; "The Man
Behind the Gun," Past Grand Chancellor
W. C. Graves of San Francisco; "The
Order Universal," Supreme Representa
tive Staton L. Carter of Fresno; "The
Guests of Avalon." Grand Tribune D. C.
Clark of Santa Cruz.
To-morrow afternoon the guests are to
enjoy a drive around the city, and In the
evening the ball at the armory takes
place.

KNIGHTS OF BRORASSAN.
Novices Make the Journey Across the
Burning Sands.
SANTA CRUZ. May 17.â Dramatic
Order Knights of Khorassan met at the
Native Sons' Hall last night, according
to a decree:
Now, therefore, it la decreed In commemora
tion of our Krood fortune po wisely ordained of
Allah that a pilgrimage be undertaken to the
oasis of the city of the Sacred Cross (Santa
<'ruz). scheduled to halt at that Mecca at the
hour of prayer, A. H. 1217, the sixth day, first
month, Mcharruni, or, as is known by the
dogs of unbelievers, Tuesday, May 16, 189 at
8 p, m.
Form ye. the caravan, your baskets fill with
wheaten leaves from ovens hot, measure to its
brim each bait with water fresh from coolest
rill. Get out and bustle, hustle, rustle and
tussle, so lh.it an army of tyros may be led
taptlve and our coffers be filled with shekels
r.nd remember the finale will depend upon your
patience, perspicacity, persistency and p. r
spimtlon.
The ceremonial session of I.erin Kap!
Temple No. 52, D. O. K. X., was held, and
thirty new members were initiated, six
of whom were from Santa Cruz. The
candidates' feet were blistered, and they
endured all sorts of hardships In crossing
the desert sands. The officers of the tem
ple are: Royal vizier, F. A. Merguire;
venerable sheik, H. Schaffner; grand
c-mlr, J. C. Lohmeyer; mahedl, Godfrey
Rueger; mokanna, George Samuels; me
rial, S. W. Powell; sahib, W. C. Loh
meyer.
RECEPTION TENDERED.
Rathbone Sisters the Guests of Her-
mion Temple.
SANTA CRUZ, May 17.-The Grand
Temple of Rathbone Sisters heard re
ports from its district chiefs and the
Krand officers this morning. Thirty-seven
temples, with seventy-five delegates, are
represented in the Grand Temple. In Cal
ifornia there are forty-nine temples, with
Â£000 members.
This evening the St. George Hotel was
the mecca of the Rathbone Sisters and
their friends at the reception given by
Hermlon Temple. The halls and parlors
presented a brilliant appearance. A fine
programme was given. During the even
ing refreshments were served.
The Grand Temple will hold a business
session to-morrow morning. In the after
noon it will enjoy a drive.

CORRIGAN HAS A GOOD DAY

ALARIA AND ADOLPH SPRECK
ALARIA AND ADOI/PH SPRECK-
ELS WON PURSES.
â +â
St. Casimir and Potente Unfortun-
ate Enough to Be Beaten â Sly
Downed Horatio in a Drive.
Four Choices- First.
The gate receipts at Oakland track for
the remaining three racing days of the
week will be contributed to charity by the
exiled Pacific Coast Jockey Club. Season
badges will not be honored. Most of the
money thus' obtained will be used to beau-
tify the boulevards leading to Ingleside
track. This is certainly a most worthy
object, and the blind, lame and Infirm
ought to turn out in large numbers. If
the Pacific Coast Jockey Club desires to
spread this money about where it will
do the most good, why, don't let it leave
the track. There are dozens of horsemen
that do not know which way to turn for
want of a dollar. For months they have
been looking despair in the face, while
race after race has been put on the pro-
gramme for Corrigan and his favored bar-
nacles. And this is the game the Califor
nia public has been asked to support.
These parasites from the Middle West can
race their horses "in and out" and noth-
ing is said. But if a California owner
"blinks his eye" he Is ordered to pack his
traps and emigrate. Charity begins at
home, and the racing association should
assist their own needy before trying to
convert the savages.
The racing yesterday had a sweet Cor-
riganesque flavor to it. Chicago grew
tired of Corrigan, so the master of Haw-
thorne immigrated to this side of the
Rockies, and has been quoted as saying
"that he liked our climate." Why
shouldn't he? During the last two weeks.
of racing his horses have won $3750. Many
of the races were "set-ups," and he bet'
liberally on them. The Derby was post
poned to give his Corsine time to recu
perate, the judges disqualified Pat Mor-
rissey one day that he might cash on
Adolph Spreckels, and several other inci
dents have happened on the quiet to make
his stay pleasant.
Yesterday Alarla and Adolph Spreckels
were returned winners, while Potente,
favorite for the handicap, struck a
snag. This latter was an unlooked for
occurrence. . In all four favorites were
successful.
The sport began with a five-furlong
scramble for two-year-olds, Giga going
to the post favorite. Jenkins bad the
mount on the Foul Shot filly and catching
St. Casimir in the sail home, won driving

Track Notes.
The fast sprinter Pelxotto died at Oakland
track yesterday from an attack of colic.
Secretary Nathanson of Harlem track, Chi
cago, wires that the betting ring is open to
all bookmakers who wish to cut in. The syndi
cate style of gambling was not a success.
Two or three bookmakers had their fingers
scorched handling Malay money. The horse was
handed about as a "dead one," but some one
registered a kick with the judges, which was
not without cheering results. Jack Ward had
the mount. = > r i

FREE CAMPING
Ideal camping spots all along the main
line, Sonoma and Guemeville branches of
the California Northwestern Railway
(lessee San Francisco and North Pacific
Railway), the picturesque route. of Cali-
fornia. While parties can camp any-
where, close to or remote from the rail-
road, a few picturesque spots especially
adapted for campers have been selected
on account of their easy access to San
Francisco.
Glen Ellen Park and four miles along
Sonoma Creek, beautifully shaded. Excel-
lent fishing and bathing. Mr. C. E. Har-
vey, at Glen Ellen, will show locations.
Mirabel Park, on . our Guemeville
branch,' adjoining the railroad, and skirted
by Mark West- Creek, which is now
dammed, affording fine bathing, boating
for two miles, and good fishing. Man in
charge. Row boats at reasonable rental.
Guemeville, in the midst of the redwood
country, and on the Russian River, is
practically the terminus of ! the Gueme-
ville branch. The people of the town and
vicinity, under the name of the Business
Men's Association, of Guemeville, offer
free camping sites throughout the whole
section. The Russian â â â  River will be
dammed, affording a magnificent sheet of
water for bathing and boating. Mr. D.
Connell. proprietor of the livery stable at
Guemeville, representing the association,
will meet all trains, and without charge
will show prospective campers the various
locations.
To and from all these . points double
daily train service.
Supplies delivered on the grounds by
the merchants of the adjoining towns.
Tickets for camping parties are to be
procured at Ticket Office, 650 Market st.
For detailed information apply at Ticket
Office, 650 Market st.. Chronicle building,

Sansome and California streets.

When the case of Newhouse against
Wiltsee. in which the plaintiff prays the
Chancellor to adjudge that he is a partner
with Wiltsee in the Providence mine, was
called yesterday afternoon the case of the
defendant proceeded. In his introduction
Attorney Charles S. Wheeler made an ef
fort to introduce testimony of, witnesses
to. the effect that Newhouse was but an
agent of Wiltsee and that as such agent
he had violated the confidence of his em
ployer and was getting a commission.
which came directly from Wilts.- 's
pocket, on all mining machinery, imple
ments, provisions and paraphernalia pur
chased by him as such agent for the
development of such mine. It was claimed
that powder was sold at. an advance to
Wiltsee, that Newhouse got a "knock
down" on drug bills and so on, the prac
tice being one. as Attorney Wheeler put
it, "of petty thieving and dishonest pil
fering." A long legal argument followed
on the relevancy of the testimony, but
finally Judge Seawell sustained an objec
tion of Garret McEnerney, who represents
Newhouse.
Mr. Wheeler contended that the matter
of "unclean hands" was one for the deter
mination of the Chancellor and that tes
timony showing that Newheuse had come
into court in the condition prohibited by
a court of equity should be admitted. In
any event. Judge Seawell sustained the
objection without prejudice, and the mat
ter will be further argued this morning.
During the course of the proceedings it
fell to Mr. McEnerney to make a counter
charge of unclean hands against Wiltsee.
Mr. McEnerney said that Wiltsee had
admitted his culpability under the maxim
on the witness stand under these circum
stances: Wiltsee and Lord Sudeley were
partners in the Drummond mine, a prop
erty in which Newhouse claims no In
terest. Wiltsee employed Newhouse to
visit the mine and perform some services,
after which the principals in the present
litigation met. Wiltsee told Newhouse
that he should certainly be paid for his
labors on the Drummond mine; "but you

Judge Troutt yesterday granted the ap
plication of the creditors of aged
"Mammy" Pleasant, she of the "house of
mystery," and declared the old woman in
solvent "Mammy" must now, against
her will, pay the demands of these cred
itors, which sum up between $50,000 and
(100,000. Mrs. Pleasant has eonsiderablo
property which she purchased while em
ployed by the unfortunate Sarah Altnea
Terry and her late ward, Mrs. Theresa
Bell. When the judgment was entered
"Mammy" was not in court, being con
fined to her bed With an attack of 'syn
cope." The proceedings In court yester
day were short but merry for the cred
itors, who are jubilant with the thought
that they can now attach the old woman *^
property and recover the" amounts dU<*T

cals appear to have a shade the best of

cidedly stronger in hitting, while in field

running as well, the Athenians have the

ought to decide to the satisfaction of all
the cranks, and both have stanch advo
cates.

THE STREET DEPARTMENT MEN
MUST BE SILENT.
The Practice of Blackmailing Men
on the City's Pay Roll Is
Said to Be a Common
One.
Publication of the facts concerning the â¢
proposed investigation by the Board of I ;
Supervisors into the affairs of the office ,
of Superintendent of Streets Fragley has â  ,
caused no end of a commotion among the ]
employes at the City Hall. All of the
attaches of the office over which Mr. ,
Fragley has control have been supenaed ;
to appear before a committee of the '
Board of Supervisors on Friday, at which
time they will be questioned as to their
knowledge of the charges that Mr. Frag- .
ley requires them to pay him a portion
of their monthly or daily salary, refusal
to make such payment being considered
sufficient cause for their dismissal from
the employ of the city. . . .
To several officials who called on him
yesterday, Mr. Fragley denied that the
charge made against him is founded on
fact and intimated that he would make
It extremely warm for such members of
the Board of Supervisors as he considered
responsible for the proposed investiga
tion. He even intimated that the charge
made against him might be made with
truth against any one of half a dozen
members of the board. In this connection |
he declared his intention of making a I
few disclosures in the event that he is
called upon to testify before the board.
It is well known to the initiated that .
at least three-quarters of the employes of
the different departments of the city
government retain their position by pay- j
ing a portion of their salaries to those
persons who secured them the positions I
or to others who are responsible for the â 
continuance of their employment. For
this reason a majority of the men on
the city's pay roll are now on the anxious :
seat because of the fear that the investi
gation that is to begin with the Superin
tendent of Streets may be extended so !
as to embrace every department of the '
city government.
It is asserted by the old timers about
the City Hall that the investigation will
be barren of results so far as Mr. Frag
ley's employes are concerned for the rea
son that they will lose their positions j
should they tell tales out of school. All
of these employes refused yesterday to
discuss the matter, but some went so |
THOSE WHO
far as to intimate that they had already
received instructions from their chief to
deny all of the charges made, or, if they
did not, they might consider their em
ployment at an end. If such instructions
have been given and they are obeyed
the Supervisors will have to look to some
other source for the information they
need to substantiate tl\e charges.
It is said by those who pretend to
know that men whose salaries are fixed
at $150 a month are required to surrender
$50 a month in order to retain their posi
tions. Men whose salaries are $100 .a
month are forced to pay $25. and those
who get $75 a month surrender $15 every
pay day. The same ratio is said to be
maintained as regards the salaries of day
laborers.

STADER'S HOME BURNED.
A Defective Chimney Destroys Two
Houses on Wilder Street.
The fire alarm from box 571 (Chenery
and Diamond) at 12:45 p. m. yesterday
was for a fire at the residence of Chris
Stader. president of the "West of Castro
Street Improvement Club, at 29 Wilder
street. In consequence of the high wind
that blew in through the creek ravine
of Glen Park, together with the distance
from the nearest engine-house, the home
of Stader was totally destroyed. The ad
joining house, which was owned by him,
but occupied as a residence by John
Hermann, was partly burned. To add to
the other misfortunes attending the lire
there was a scarcity of water in the
neighborhood, and it was with difficulty
that the firemen were enabled to fori
It to the desired object.
The buildings were insured ' for $1400.
which amount does not nearly cover the
loss. , :v; â '.'â¢:â ' :>.'â  V

A LIVELY MIX-UP OVER AN UN-

Â«
Peckerman Wanted to Take Out a
Peckerman Wanted to Take Out a
Diamond Ring for a Friend, but Re-
used to Pay the Customary
Rate of Interest.
A rather lively row occurred yesterday i
; afternoon in the pawnshop at 19 Third !
j street, recently conducted by Simon Peck- |
erman. Peckerman's son "Benny" was bad- I
ly damaged by Eugene Eber. a young man j
who. is employed at the pawnshop. The i
elder Peckerman has conducted the pawn-
shop for many years, but last week he
failed, and those who know the inside of
the old man's affairs declare that his
business and fortune were ruined by the j
.waywardness. of his two sons, Joseph and ;
Benny, both of â whom are well known on !
the coast.
Yesterday afternoon Benny Peckerman
entered the store and handed the clerk a
ticket for a diamond ring, which he
wanted to redeem at 2 per cent interest.
The regular rate is 6 per cent, and Eber
WILL LEAD
flatly refused to allow Peckerman to re
deem the ring unless he paid the cus
tomary rate of interest.
"This ring belongs to a particular friend
of mine," shouted Peckerman.
"I don't care who it belongs to. If you
want to take it out you'll have to pay
the customary rate of interest," retorted
Eber hotly.
Then Peckerman attempted to strike
Eber. The blow slipped around the clerk's
neck and the two clinched. For a min
ute or two they furnished some real good
hand-to-hand fighting. 'hairs were
knocked over, tables were upturned and
several layers of rings were scattered on
the floor. .
When the police entered to separate the
pair Eber had Benny's red head under his
ferm and was pounding It severely. Young
Peckerman was game, however, and as
two husky policemen led him out of the
store he struggled hard to get free so he
could resume the. scrap.

rescue his father from prison writes
up his thrilling. experience and pub
lishes the photographs he took, in
next Sunday's Call.
Boyd's Valuable Services.
The action- of Belle Curtis and Lizzie
Mulr. heirs of the estate of the. late Jacob
Z. Davis, against Alexander Boyd, junior
partner of the old firm of, Davis &,Bovd.
, to recover $30,000 which the heirs allege. is
' due them from the defendant as the share
. of Jacob Z. Davis in the profits of the
firm since Mr. Davis' death, went to trial
before Judge Belcher yesterday. Mr.
Boyd . denies such an amount is due. but
In his pleadings practically acknowledges
that the profits amount to the sum
; stated: but he claims the entire amount
i for his services on behalf of Davis' in-
terests. Figures produced in court yester-
day show that just prior to Davis' death
he had $1,059,187 64 Invested in the firm,
while Boyd had the sum of $969,000 in-
vested, showing an overdraft on his part
! for a large sum. But little testimony was
taken, the case being. continued until this
: morning to admit of a further examina-
I tion of the books.

Strange Hallucination of a Woman.
Jennie Eichler, a well-dressed young
woman,, walked into the office of the Chief
of Police yesterday and asked to see her
parents, whom she designated as the
King and Queen. When questioned, the
woman said that her mother and father
had been arrested for betraying God and
she was anxious to see them. Convinced
that the visitor was mentally deranged.
Chief Lees ordered her locked up in the
detention ward of the Receiving Hospital
pending an examination as to her sanity.

R. H. Plummer's Will.
i The will of Richard H. Plummer, who
died April 23, has been filed for probate.
The estate of the deceased consists of real
and personal property valued at over
$10,000. To his brother. Benjamin T. Plum
mer. the deceased leaves $40 a month dur
ing that brother's life; to his sister, Ann
E. Pepperd. $1000; the residue is devised
to the testator's widow. Belle H. Plum
mer, who is named as executrix to serve
without bonds. '
