Chief Lees Unwillingly Receives the
Chief Lees Unwillingly Receives the
Affidavit, but a New One Will
Be Filed With Police
Commissioners.
.
Andrew Furuseth, secretary of the
Andrew Furuseth, secretary of the
Sailors' Union of the Pacific, and Edward
Rosenberg, secretary of the Labor Coun
cil and business manager of the Coast
Seamen's Journal, filed an affidavit last
night with Chief of Police Lees, charging
Sergeant Stephen Bunner of the harbor
police with unofficorlike conduct. The
charge may cause Sergeant Bunner to
lose his star.
The affidavit recites that on May 12 in
formation was received by the union that
some sailors wanted to leave the schooner
Ellen> A, then lying off Meiggs wharf.
Accordingly a committee composed of
three members of the unionâ Pear
son, A. Ekman and M. Crowellâ out
in a small boat to inquire into the matter.
While they were parleying with the
mate Sergeant Bunner came alongside in
the police boat. According to tne affi
davits filed, Bunner wanted to know what
the trouble was. Upon being informed,
he asked the mate if the three men were
wanted on board. The mate said they
were not and Bunner, it is alleged, began
to abuse them in forcible terms. It is
charged he told the mate to throw them
overboard and shoot a hole' through their
boat it they did not leave in a hurry.
Upon Pearson remonstrating with him, it
is stated, Bunner threatened "to smash
his face in" and to do other bodily harm.
The men thereupon got back in the boat
and pulled away.
In discussing the affair last night Mr.
Furuseth said: "We at first intended to
present the affidavit to the Police Com
missioners, but as they did not meet last
night we filed it with Chief Lees, be
lieving that would do as well. We were
soon undeceived, however, for Chief Lees
gave us scant satisfaction. He very un
willingly received the affidavit and ad-"
vised us to take the matter to the courts.
He said we had no business to bring it
to him or the Commissioners. He seemed
to think a sailor has no right to quit a
vessel whan he wants to. However, we
will prepare a new affidavit and present
it to the Commissioners at their next
meeting, and they may listen to our com
plaint." *'' -;â¢; :y-; *

It is the only offering of unimproved property ' having a great prospective
value that has taken place in years.
Every lot in Glen Park Terrace has a fine sunny exposure and commands a
good view of this new and popular resort, Glen Park.
It is an admitted fact that all property adjacent to public or private parks
enhances rapidly in value.
It Is also conceded that no park has ever attained such popularity within
- the same time as Glen Park.
What Woodward's Garden was to the city years ago so Is Glen Park to-day*
The attendance at Glen Park Dewey day was 41.257. Events to take place
! there in the near future will draw fully 100.000 people In a single day.
Wherever such crowds go real estate values must increase.
Glen Park Terrace commands the key to the whole situation.
It Glen Park and fronts on the electric road, being at the terminus
of the park branch and at the main entrance. The Market-street Railway Com-
pany is now constructing the Bosworth and Berkshire streets road into Glen
Park, and the terminus of this road will be within a few hundred feet of the lots
we offer at this sale.

Expects to Join Lawton's Command
When He Arrives at Manila.
Will 'Leave Monday on
the Sherman.
Brigadier General Frederick D. Grant
arrived last night from San Juan, Porto
Rico, en route to Manila, where he will
report to General Otis for duty.
General Grant went immediately upon
, his arrival to the Palace Hotel, where he
was greeted by his brothers, Ulysses S.
I Grant Jr. and Jesse R. Grant, who ar
j rived on the "Owl" yesterday morning
j from San Diego. He has been- travelingl
! almost continuously since the 20th of last
j month, when he left San Juan for Wash
i ington, and will spend the short time he
' has before the sailing of the transport
â  Sherman, to which be is assigned for pas
; sage to the Philippines, in absolute rest.
The general spoke interestingly of his
experience at Porto Rico, where for many
months he was in comand of the Ameri
can troops, and had charge of the re
habilitation of the governmental func
tions of the island. Concerning the peo
ple among whom he has spent so much
of his time he said:
"The Porto Ricans are an amiable, al
though somewhat excitable people and
will, I think, make good citizens after
they have had a -year or so of schooling
under our Government. They have lived
for years, of course, under Spanish rule
and Spanish customs, but they are quick
to adapt themselves to new conditions
and are taking kindly to American cus
toms.
"So far as they have experienced Amer
ican rule, however, 1 think they are
slightly disappoined. They welcomed us
as deliverers at first and were led to ex
pect radical and immediate reforms and
the reforms have evidently not come
quick enough for them. Because of the
war they neglected their crops and their
business interests generally, and- having
to pay two duties on what they were
prepared to ship out the price oh their
products was reduced to a figure lower
than that they received from Spain.
"They were In a good frame of mind to
be disappointed and they certainly are,
though they make no open demonstration
of it, and I think will be as good natured
as ever after the magnitude of proposed
American reforms become apparent to
them.
"Although we had a great deal of sick
ness among our troops, we found the cli
mate one that our people could adapt
themselves to quite readily. The country
is productive of profitable crops and a
couple of years of American rule will
make it as good a state as most of those
in the Union."
General Grant entered the army as col
onel, commanding the Fourteenth Volun
; teer Infantry of New York. He took his
regiment to Chickamauga and was com
missioned brigadier general and placed
; in command of the first division of the
Third Army Corps. With his command
he was ordered to San Juan on July 27,
i find since that time has conducted affairs
on the island. .-.-.
The Sherman will leave about the 22d
of the present month, and the general
expects to be attached to General Law
ton's command upon his arrival on the
1 other side.
Hotop Must Answer,
Frederick Hotop. the conductor on the
California street line who had vitriol
thrown in his face by Maggie Magee
Monday, appeared before Judge Graham
yesterday on the charge of betrayal un
der the promise of marriage. He was
represented by Attorney J. N. E. Wilson,
who asked for a continuance till May 23,
which was granted.
A Murderous Cripple.
Joseph Burns, a mendicant who has
ost the use of both legs, appeared be
ore Judge Mogan yesterday to answer
charge of assault with a deadly weapon
n trying to cut S. J. Sims, 149 Fifth
treet. with a knife. He agreed to plead
polity to battery and the Judge convicted
dm and sentenced him to three months
n the County Jail.

A NATIONAL LABEL LAW.
O CCORDING to the testimony of experts be-
ACCORDING to States Senate of Pure Food
fore the United States Senate Pure Food
Commission, there is hardly an article of food
that is not made the subject of some form of decep
tion upon consumers. Dr. H. W. Wiley, chief of the
division of chemistry in the Agricultural Department,
is reported to have informed the commission, "Prac
tically everything we eat or drink is/ adulterated."
Other witnesses gave testimony that substantially con
firmed the statement.
It is conceded that while many of these adultera
tions are harmful and even dangerous to health and
life, a considerable number are not. There is also an
intermediate class, said to be harmless by the
adulterators who make use of them, but regarded as
harmful by physicians. Salicylic acid is one of this
class, as the adulterators claim it is used in such small
quantities it cannot possibly injure any one. while
the weight of medical authority is adverse to the
claim, the experience of the profession being that
when used to adulterate any article of common use
so that the consumer uses it habitually even in small
quantities, the consequences are pernicious in the ex
treme.
One of the salient features of the evidence sub
mitted to the commission is that while European
Governments forbid the sale of adulterated foods
within their own territory, they do not forbid the
manufacture of such goods for export. The result
is the United States are made a dumping ground for
all kinds of European concoctions as well as those
made at home. The evil has become one of por
tentous magnitude, and it is full time some efficient
remedy were provided for it. It is the design of the
Senate Commission to recommend the passage of
some law to that effect, and the subject is now being
discussed extensively by the Eastern press.
The prevailing tone of our Eastern exchanges, we
regret to say. is not sanguine. As the Philadelphia
Record expresses it: '.'Whether the Federal legisla
tive authority could be induced to undertake seriously
the task of reforming the evil practices of the legion
of food adulterators is open to question, since the
public interest counts for but little in Congress when
weighed against the influences exerted by a powerful
lobby. But if there be any desire to legislate for the
interests of the consumer it should be easy to demon
strated the lawmakers the fairness and justice of a
law which would enable buyers of articles of food to
know exactly what they were paying for. A truthful
label on an adulterated article would infallibly drive
it from the market in due time, no matter how
'cheap' it might be."
There can be little doubt of the essential soundness
of that view of the case. A law requiring all articles
to be correctly labeled as to their nature and the
place and time of manufacture would injure â¢ no
honest industry. Such a law is so fair to all that it
would seem impossible for even the most powerful
lobby to defeat it if a strong public demand should
be made for it.
Russell Sage agrees. with C. P. Huntington that
the education of the young in this country is too gen
eral. Both of the fox-like financiers want the young
men. of America to leave their books for "practical"
life. Is it possible that the old gentlemen wish to
educate American boys down to their standards of
business and professional ethics? One Sage in a
generation is enough. One Huntington is more than
enough.
That some people in England have objected to
Choate's declaration that Dewey is as great as Nel
son is not surprising. In this country there would be
some objection if an English orator should as
sert Nelson to be as great as Dewey. In the game
of favorites each nation is entitled to its own.
To save the fruit industry, from the domination of
a great trust is going to take a hard fight and a long
one, but the thing can be done by co-operation, and
fortunately the fruit-growers have had experience
enough to know how to combine against a combine
as well as anybody. -
If Funston be wise he will stay where he is for at
least a year. His friends in Kansas have already de
vised forty kinds of temptation to make a Kansan fool
of him.
In promising to the rank and file of the party
everything in sight the local Democratic bosses would
have made a better appearance of generosity if there
had been anything in sight worth having.
The Cuban soldiers of General Gomez have fallen
upon evil times. The dusky general intends to issue
â a manifesto commanding his followers to lay down
their arms and go to work. , ;.
A MONUMENT FOR DEWEY.
A MOVEMENT has been started the. East to
provide Admiral Dewey with a stately, mansion
house and home at the national capital. It is
right that this should be so. The nation owes much
to the Admiral, and since our laws do not admit the
large rewards which monarchical Governments, and
notably that of Great Britain, give to great men, it
is but proper a grateful and a generous people should
undertake the duty themselves and by free gift pro
vide the hero with a home worthy of his dignity and
his honors. Â
Admiral Dewey, however, merits more than a
home. His service should be commemorated by
something of a monumental character which will
stand for ages and perpetuate the memory of the
gratitude and admiration which his fellow citizens of
this generation feel toward him. There is no reason
why the Admiral should not receive a monument for
his fame as well as a home for his family. He. has
merited both, and the wealth and the liberality of the
American people are equal to the patriotic task of
providing both.
The home of the Admiral is to be in the East, his
noblest monument should be in the West. His man-
sion is to be in Washington, let his memorial statue
stand in San Francisco. The people of, the Eastern

. Most of the physical labor in the Philippines now
is performed by Chinese coolies, imported under con-
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL. THURSDAY, MAY 18, 1899.
tract. If we keep the islands and give them a semi-
nnlitary administration to hold the people in subjec
tion, the order enforced by the sword' will be taken
advantage of by capitalists to import and use coolie
labor under penal contract. The American army will
be handy to coerce labor and compel the Asiatic
slaves to perform their contracts. At this moment
we see the army being used at home to compeT em-
ployers to accept certain .conditions, and to forbid
labor the right to earn wages unless under certificate
from the commanding officer!
/- . - â¢
Granting all the errors and crimes to which folly
has led laboring men, their effect was transitory", and
the civil courts could deal with them. But .when the
army fixes the conditions upon which men may work
at home, what may be expected of, military adminis
tration 7000 miles away!
The tropics offer no shelter, nor prosperity, nor
health, nor length of days, to the American home-
seeker. It is said that these new possessions will
serve to drain off the increasing class of the, discon-
tented among us. There is never discontent without
a cause. Deporting those who suffer from that
cause does not remove it, but rather tends to its in-
crease. A small part of the hundreds of millions
spent in tropical exploitation already, if devoted to
home concerns, would have left no American sus
ceptible to the lure of home-seeking in the tropics.
JUDICIAL LEGISLATION.
r V
THE body of the common law, though in theory
I derived from immemorial custom, is in reality
chiefly of judicial creation. The English
Judges, accepting certain principles, rules and maxims
as fundamental, extended their application from cen
tury to century, so that in effect they exercised legis
lative authority. 'They have always charged juries on
matters of fact, which has given them a vast influence
in the determination of practical issues. Under ..the
arbitrary rule of the Stuarts, when the Star Chamber
was in existence, the last batch of corrupt and arbi
trary Judges known in English history dictated ver
dicts and held life and liberty almost completely at
their mercy.
Judge-made law, however irregular in its origin,
accomplished beneficent results under the great Lord
Mansfield, who drew from all available sources, in
cluding the civil law, of which English Judges had
been profoundly jealous, and adapted and expanded
commercial jurisprudence to the necessities of modern
times.
But when the Government of the United States j
was established, the wise statesmen and patriots of j
our country, appreciating the terrible danger to free {
institutions in the centralization of power and in the I
blending of two dissimilar functions in the judiciary, j
in the first place accurately defined the limitations on I
Federal authority, by holding it within an express |
grant and essential implications, and, in the second
place, as a fundamental proposition, organized three
separate governmental departments, each distinct from \
the others, while all were connected by the indis- j
soluble bond of the Union. Thus it happens that j
from 1787 to the present time, in theory and until late i
years substantially in practice, the jurisdiction and
procedure in the Federal courts have been rigidly
confined within the text of enabling statutes and such I
implied powers as were indispensable to the effectual i
administration of justice within those carefully de- i
fined limits. The constitutional amendments result- ;
ing from the Civil War, justly designed to insure per- j
j sonal liberty and the protection of property, greatly
I increased the jurisdiction of the Federal courts, j
They have always exercised the anomalous right of ;
expounding evidence before juries, which has oper- i
I ated to insure or defeat justice according to the in- '
1 dividual training, opinions and practice of the incum
' bents of the Federal bench. The people, however.
1 have been so jealous of judicial encroachments; the
j tendency to which is natural and marked, that the
j State constitutions, almost if not quite unanimously,
j have distinctly prohibited that kind of interference
with jury trials.
The gradual enlargement of the powers of the Fed-
eral judiciary and its increasing exercise of legisla
tive functions of late years has been facilitated by
Congressional enactments that favor s concentrated
and aggressive wealth, at the expense of the rooted
principles .of American institutions. No more for-
midable or dangerous example of this tendency could
be found than part of the decision' of Judge McKenna
jin the grain rate case, as interpreted by Railroad I
! Commissioner Blackstock in his report, ultimately
[ perfected with the assistance of William F. Herrin.
The enormous -advantage to be derived by the rail-
road and by other oppressive monopolies from the
views thus disseminated can be readily perceived from
a 1 short extract: "While the commission has a con-
stitutional and statutory authority to regulate and
fix freights and fares, yet the same must be just and
reasonable, and the question as to whether or not they
are just and reasonable may be investigated and de-
termined by a court of competent jurisdiction"â that
is, by a Federal tribunal. It will be observed that the
law here declared is not that the court, may decide
whether a State commission has exercised some intelli
: gence and some discretion In the adjustment of rates,
; but whether the' rates as' established are "just and
reasonable." The ultimate right, therefore, to deter- !
j mine the main fact within the conceded jurisdiction of
; the commission is claimed to have been transferred to ; :
the Circuit Court of the United States. This means j
I that a Federal Judge can exercise the identical func- j
I tions of a railroad commission, and if his judgment I
approve the schedule, then it will stand, but other-
wise it will be set aside.
It is doubtful whether, in the exact case to which
we have adverted, on a final hearing the Circuit Court !
or the Supreme Court of the United States would !
justify this wholesale appropriation of/authority. But j
the tendency, somewhat weaker now than three or |
! four years ago, is unquestionably in that direction j
) and should be corrected by wise Congressional legis- j
j lation, equally just to corporations and to the people, I
i and equally restraining the illegal dominancy of par- j
j ticular classes of monoplies and the anarchical ele- \
i ment that has apparently taken possession of the !
j Democratic party. In the State courts, in reference
to mutual benefit and benevolent societies, recent de
cisions. have almost made them independent of the es
tablished tribunals of the country, and there are Fed-
eral decisions somewhat on the same line, indicating
that great jurists have at least scented the danger we
have pointed out.. But the possibility of an "unnatural
commingling of judicial , and .legislative. \ functions
should be averted by clear and definite statutes, leav
ing, no room for misconstruction: There can be no
more vital necessity- for the safety of the republic
than firm adherence to the original distribution Â» of
powers between the three departments â of govern-
ment. , Â:.,,, , >,-â¢â  *.
The only satisfactory explanation of the objection
of Huntington and Russell Sage to liberal education
is to be found in the fact that neither of them has
ever been either liberal or educated.
The Rev. Dr. Charles A. Briggs is at last an or
dained Episcopal minister. Perhaps his friends will
now permit him to mind his own business. " ' ""
ELMHURST'S NEW DIVINE
Rev. Edward E. Clark, Who Has Accepted a Call
From the First Presbyterian Church.
REV. EDWARD E. CLARK, the well-known divine, has accepted a
call from the Presbyterian church at Elmhurst.
Dr. Clark was born in West Virginia, near Steubenville, Ohio. He
has a twin brother, also a Presbyterian pastor in Southwestern Penn
sylvania. He was a graduate of the Steubenville High School of Ohio. He
was also an honor man in the class of 1883 from Marietta College and from
the Lane Theological Seminary, Cincinnati,', class of 1886.
Dr". Clark's first pastorate was at Cambridge City, Ind. He has been
eleven years in California, having had charge of Presbyterian churches in
Berkeley. Salinas, Monterey and' Fruitvale. His predecessor at Elmhurst
church .was Rev. R. C. Stone, now pastor of the Centennial Presbyterian
Church, East Oakland.
A SAN FRANCISCAN RUNS
THE LAGUNA DE BAI
Sergeant Henry F. Jurs, Chief Engineer of the
Famous Tin-Clad Gunboat.
ONE of the men from San Francisco who has distinguished himself at
the Philippines In the war with the Insurgents Is Henry F. Jurs, who
enlisted in the war with Spain from the signal corps of the -Second
Brigade of the National Guard of California and entered the service
as a member of Company Aof the Volunteer Signal Corps. The particular
line in which he became prominent was cheif engineer, of the armored United
States gunboat Laguna de Bai, the vessel which has become famous as the
one that cleared the Pasig River as far as the lake. .
Jurs, who, in recognition of his valuable services, was . appointed to be
first-class sergeant, the highest non-commissioned. position: he could reach,
at the time of his enlistment, lived with his parents at IS Johnstone avenue.
He is a native of this city, 26 years of age, and . was employe/! at the Union
Iron Works, where he served m various departments to become a. mechani
cal engineer. At the works he was held in high esteem, on account of his
proficiency in every branch in which he served. aifeHBBgSBBBi
At the suggestion of a man named Mead, sent from the Union Iron
Works to put captured Spanish vessels in condition for the use of the forces
at Manila,^Sergeant Jurs was selected to assist him in armoring the Laguna
de Bai and to put her engines in serviceable condition, being detailed from
his command for that purpose. After the vessel had been put in fighting
trim, Filipino and other engineers were placed in charge of the engine room.
but five who were tried could not make the vessel come up to the standard
that was required. Then- by order of General Otis Sergeant Jurs was de
tailed as chief engineer. B&6*aÃ
How well he succeeded has been told in the various dispatches that
have been published in The Call about the extraordinary cruise of the lit
tle iron-covered steamer that so terrified the natives who were within gun-
shot of her.. A tribute to. his -worth In the position he occupies -was paid him
by Captain Grant,, the commander of the vessel, who, on introducing him
to the first lieutenant of the, Utah Battery, said: "This- Is Sergeant: Jurs
the young -man who is our chief engineer, and who has made our gunboat
successful when everybody else tailed. I shall do what I can to secure a
commission for him," for he deserves it, if anybody ever did."*
TRANSPORTATION FOR RETURNING SOLDIERS.
; To the Editor of The Call: You .were kind enough to publish a communica
tion from me some time ago upon the injustice of compelling the marines and blue
jackets coming home on the transport Buffalo to pay their own fares overland to
their respective places of abode and enlistment.
. I have reason to knotf that the department at Washington 'took cognizance of
that very same published complaint and acted upon it immediately. The paper
containing the article was taken by a personal friend, to the proper authority and
the result was announced in the dispatches three days later that all of them
would be furnished transportation to their various homes. This saves my son
and many others the payment of railroad fare across the continent to this city,
where they enlisted. .
They all owe many thanks. to The Call for its kindness and great* influence.
Yours truly, JAMES W. COLLINS,
San Francisco, May 13, 1899. 54 Sixth street.

THE GAS COMPANYâ C. 0.. and
A. S., City. On the subject of a gas
company furnishing gas to applicants the
Civil Code of California, section 629, says:
Upon application in writing of the owner or
occupant of any building or premises distant
not more than one hundred feet from any main
of the corporation and payment by the appli
cant of all money due from him to
the coropration, the corporation must
supply gas for such building or prem
ises and cannot, refuse on the ground of
any Indebtedness of any' former owner or
occupant thereof, unless the applicant has un
dertaken to pay the same. If. for the space of
ten days after such application; the corpora
tion refuses or neglects to supply the -gas, it
must pay to the applicant the sum of $50 in
liquidated damages, and $5 a day for every day
such refusal or neglect continues thereafter.
It has been decided that a gas company can
not, under the law, demand a deposit from a.
consumer or one who applies to consume gasi-
That case is, however, on appeal. . . Â

MECHANICS OFF
FOR PAGO PAGO
COAL STATION
Tibbits Goes on the
Moana.
â â
COMPETENT MEN WITH HIM
â Â» â
PREPARE TO REMAIN IN THE
PREPARE TO REMAIN IN THE
SOUTH SEAS OVER A YEAR.
â¦
Have No Fear the Natives Will In-
terrupt Operations on the Big
Undertaking for This
Government.
When the Moana sailed for Australia
yesterday by way of Samoa she took with
her the headquarters' staff of the Pago
Pago baling station expedition. The con
tractors. Healy. Tibbits & Co. of
this City, have now completed all their
arrangements for commencing work on
this most important strategical position.
They are bound by their contract with
the United States Government to erect
within the present year a steel wharf run
ning out over the reef Into deep water.
as well as coal sheds ashore capable of
holding at least 5000 tons. In addition
there will be a caretaker's house and va
rious subsidiary buildings, all of which
will bring Uncle Sam's expenditure on
this station to considerably over $200,000.
"There is a great deal of work to do
and but little time in which to do it." re
marked "General" Tibbits yesterday, as
he sat in close consultation with his engi
neers and draughtsmen over the plans for
the pier. "In consequence we have had
to hasten our preparations.
"When we took this contract," con-
â¢inued Tibbits, "deep peace brooded over
.he land and we thought we had an easy
Job. But now since the war has devel-
oped we do not know . exactly how we
stand. We have applied to Washington
and the naval authorities have promised
us ample protection while we are carry-
ing on the work. The armed collier Bru
tus is there and wfil probably be detailed
as a guard ship at Pago Pago."
Mr. Tibbits takes with him on the Mo-
ana a force of thirty-five skilled artisans.
including two supervising engineers,
three foremen, fifteen steel workers, five
carpenters and* ten structural steel build-
ers. . Lieutenant F. T. Chambers, United
States navy, accompanies the expedition
to represent the Government's interests:
He Is accompanied by two engineers as
assistants, G. A. Mackay and E. R. Gay-
lor.. These gentlemen will see. to it that
the pier and coaling station are erected
strictly in accordance with the specifica
tions, and that the country gets full
value for its 1200.000.
"We have arranged our dates." said
the general, "so that If all goes well we
should be able to commence operations
upon the arrival of the Moana. We
should land in Apia on June 1, and my
first step will be to see the United States
Special Commissioner, Bartlett Tripp,
who Is in Samoa. If possible I will ar-
range to have our torce taken to Pago
Pago on a United States war vessel, as
that would have an excellent effect on the
wild natives of Tutuda. We are taking
with us provisions and all the materials
for house building. We have no fear of
trouble with the natives. As long as we
are under the protection of a man-of-
war's guns they will be hardly likely to
attack us."" v". â¢â¢'-â¢.
The contractors have had to take their
chances of the risks and delays of the
sea. A storm or shipwreck may at any
t'me upset all their carefully arranged
plans. For instance, their plant and ma-
terial for construction is on three dif
ferent vessels. The United States collier
Abarenda sailed on the first of this
month from an Eastern port, having on
board some 2000 tons of steel piling manu
factured by Carnegie at Pittsburg. She
Is due at â¢ Apia about the middle of
August. The Cardigan Castle, a sailing
vessel, left San Francisco on .April 25,
carrying all the machinery ana con-
tractors' plant, as well as a large quan
tity of Iron castings made in this city.
She should arrive, if she makes a good
passage, about the same time as Mr. Tib-
bits and his party.
In spite of the fact that the greater
portion of the steel work had, because of
excessive railroad rates, to be made in
the East. San Francisco is not going to
do so badly out of this contract. At least
one-half of the - $200,000 will be spent
directly or indirectly in this city. The
wages bill alone will come to more than
J20.000. Altogether some fifty or sixty men
will be gent from here at wages ranging
from 's9o to $150 per month. The men are
guaranteed at least eight months' con
stant work, and probably the term of op
erations may extend over a year.

Not Captain Stensland.
Captain Stensland of the schooner Mary
and Ida is nor' the man charged with re
fusing to land a sailor with a broken leg
at Puget Sound. The injured man was
mate of the schooner Uranus and said
the captain's name was Stensland, but In
stead of that it was Stangeland. Captain
Stensland is well and favorably known on
the coast. He has been a shipmaster here
for twenty years and has never had any
trouble with any of his crews.

Off for the Rookeries.
â¢ The Fish Commission has decided 10
send another expedition against the sea
lions on Friday or Saturday. This move
will be upon the herds at the Ano Nuevo
Islands' down the coast. It will :be under
the direction of Captain J. C. Scott, ac
companied by Emil Sandtran and Alfred
Dahl. The second expedition will be
headed by A. W. Wilson, with L. XV. XV
Green and A. G. Maddren. a student at
Stanford, and 3. O.'Snuder. assistant to
Professor Gilbert' of that college. This
party will go to the Farallones -as soon as
they are able to pack their traps and get
away. The scientific gentlemen will make
a careful investigation as to the habits,
food, etc., of the sea lions of the coast.

zebue Sound and Point Hope. For all these places she has freight
and passengers, and as soon as Captain Cogan has landed them he will
go on a whaling cruise.

ACADEMIC LEAGUE RACES.
Second Annual Tournament at Olym-
pic Track Next Saturday.
The Academic Athletic League will hold
its second annual bicycle tournament at
the Olympic track next Saturday after
noon. Each school in the league is al
lowed teams of five men, but no man can
enter in more than two events. The
races will be a half-mile, mile and five
mile on singles, two-mile pursuit and
one-mile tandems. Points will be al
lowed for the three best positions at the
finish, and the prises. to the winners will
be gold and silver medals. >
There are nearly twenty-five schools In
the league, and some of the best amateur
riders in the State belong to them. The
Santa Rosa High, Berkeley High and the
San Jose High Schools have the most fa
vorable chances, but they will be hard
pressed by the Lowell team. Polytechnic
High. Lick. Oakland and Central.
The following committee has charge of
the affair: H. Hauser,, XV. A. E. Woods
and H. A. Keeler. Entries will probably
be announced to-morrow.
A Children's Playground.
The directors of the Merchants' Asso
ciation yesterday considered the annual
meeting of the association to be held next
Monday evening at -the Academy of
Sciences Hall. It was decided to make
the question of "Children's Public Play
grounds" the special subject for this
meeting. The California Club, composed
of many prominent ladies in San Fran
cisco, having made this subject a special
study it was decided to invite that or
ganization to attend the meeting and to
have several, lady members address the
meeting upon "Children's Public Play
grounds." This question has become im
portant from the fact that other cities
have made provision for children's public
playgrounds. It is expected that the dis
cussion will prove ooth interesting and
Instructive. The public Is' cordially In
vited to attend.
Fertitta Acquitted.
Felipl Fertltta, who on February 24 last
stabbed and killed Joe Slerre, was ac
quitted of the charge of murder against
him by a jury in Judge Cook's court yes
terday afternoon.. Reel B. Terry, who
represented the accused, established the
fact that the accused acted in self-de
fense, and so the Jury, exonerated him.

The mail steamer Moana sailed for
Sydney, N. S. '.W., via Honolulu, H. 1.,
Apia. Samoa, and Auckland, N. Z., yes
terday. She took away an unusually
heavy cargo and every Inch of passenger
accommodation, was taken. Mail for the
Philippines went down on the steamer
and at Honolulu it will be transferred to
one of the transports.
A package of dispatches for the Ameri
can Minister and another for the British
Commissioner were put aboard the Mo
ana during the afternoon. They will be
delivered at Apia. There were also ad
vices for Admiral Kautz and Captain
Sturdee. The mail boat will not reach
Samoa until about June 7, and by that
time the representatives of America.
Great Britain and Germany will have got
things pretty well straightened out.
There was very nearly a serious con
flagration at Lombard street wharf yes
terday. A kettle of tar boiled over and
in a few minutes the structure was in a
blaze. A big pile of lumber with which
the wharf was to be repaired was close
by and in a pen adjoining were a number
of cattle and a drove of pigs. A telephone
message was sent to Captain Brown,
superintendent of tugs and dredges, and
inside of twenty minutes he had the fire
boat Governor Markham playing a stream
of water on the blaze. It was a wonder
fully quick piece of work and It saved
the Lombard street wharf and Shed.
About three years ago the Folsom street
wharf was set afire by a pot of tar boil
ing over, but on that occasion the struc
ture was destroyed.
An interesting race between the barken
tine Wrestler and brig Lurllne Is now on.
There .ire only four, berths at Kahului
and it is a case of first come first served.
Hence the race. Both vessels were to
have sailed yesterday, but Captain Niel
son of the Wrestler stole a . march and
got away Tuesday. In order to do so he
had to leave some of his freight behind
and hunt up a mate during the night.
Captain McLeod took his time and got
away at the appointed hour. He says he
can give the wrestler twenty-four hours'
start' and still beat her. When last seen
the Lurllne was literally flying under
every stitch of canvas toward Hawaii.
The first of .the fleet for the gold fields
will get away about May 22. The bark
Alaska is now loading and as soon as
all the freight is aboard she will sail.
Nearly all the passenger accommodations
are taken and the stout old craft will be
filled from stem to stern. The first stop
ping place of the Alaska will be St. Law
rence Island. From there she will go to
Golovin Bay, thence to Cape Nome. I'n
alaklik. Cape Prince of Wales. Kotzebue
Sound and Point Hope. . For all these
places she has passengers and freight,
and as soon as they are landed Captain
Cogan will go out on a whaling cruise.
He will keep the bark out on that lay
for a few months and will then come
back to San Francisco, calling at Capo
Nome on the way.
The tug Relief arrived in port yester
day after her unsuccessful search for the
Ellhu Thomson. Captain. Randall says
they experienced nothing hut , heavy
weather all the time the tug was out.
When the transport Valencia arrived In
port she had a stowaway aboard named
George Pavenot. After leaving Nagasaki
he was discovered and put to work.
When the Valencia got here the Immi
gration Commissioner refused to allow
Pavenot to land, as he is a pauper and
cannot speak a word of English. Pave
not is supposed to be a deserter from
the Russian navy and will be Sent back
to Nagasaki on the next China steamer.
John Oilman, a veteran of the Civil
War, was found in an unconscious condi
tion on Clay street yesterday and re
moved, to the Harbor Hospital. The sur
geons who ; examined him found he was
paralyzed on the left side. There is. a
very slight chance of his recovery.
Referee in Bankruptcy.
E. :B. Martinelli has been appointed a
referee in bankruptcy for Marin County,
with bonds at $500.
Advances made on furniture and pianos, with
or without removal. J. Noonan, 1017-1023 Mission.

Governor Gage has planned to go south
for a session of reflection. One of his
most ardent and active supporters in the
campaign last November made the re
mark at the Palace last evening that the
Governor was going home to reflect how
it happened that he had made himself in
a few months the most unpopular man In
California. There is a notion among the
Southern California politicians that Gen
eral Harrison Gray Otis, with a club, is
waiting for Gage at . the Los Angeles
County line.
It is expected that the Governor will
shortly appoint three Port Wardens.
Among the men mentioned are A. J.
Marten, State Senator F. W. Burnett and
Assemblyman C. F. Kenneally.
The Governor has not yet made public
the names of the three menâ two Republi
cans and one Democratâ to comprise the
Paris Exposition Commission. The public
has been assured that Ben C. Truman is
appointed, and that the little formality
of a commission duly signed by the Gov
ernor Is all that prevents him from ex
ercising the authority and enjoying the
emoluments of a Commissioner. The
story Is not true that W. W. Foote has
been appointed to the commission. The
Governor has announced a purpose to ap
point a big Democrat from San Francisco,
and the talk is common that Mr. Foote
can have the office if he desires it. Yes
terday Mr. Foote, in speaking of the com
mon rumor, said that he had not ex
changed a word with the Governor on
that subject. The leading politicians are
of the opinion that Samuel Rucker of
San Jose, the prospective son-in-law of
Major Frank McLaughlin, will not be ap
pointed to the commission. The politi
cians are trying to guess the name of
the Northern California Republican that
has been slated for a place on the Expo
sition Board.
TRANSPORT PORTLAND IN PORT
Private Jeremiah Shea of the Califor-
nia Heavy Artillery Dies at Sea.
The United States army transport Port
land arrived In port last night, twenty-
four days out from Manila. She brought
twenty discharged soldiers, all privates.
Three days after leaving Manila Private
Jeremiah Shea of the California Heavy
Artillery died of dysentery and was buried
|at sea off the . island of Formosa. The
voyage was -uneventful and all on board
I are reported well. The Portland brings
! no freight.

Logan and His Club.
John Logan, the poundman, who is
charged with having assaulted Charles
Mitchell, a Kearny-street saloon-keeper,
with a club during a row over a dog, was
placed on trial before Judge Lawlor yes
terday on a charge of assault with a
deadly weapon. Logan pleads self-de
fense, while Mitchell claims that the as
sault was unprovoked and unnecessary.

GIVES FREELY TO THE
ACADEMY OF SCIENCES
John W. Hendree Presents $10,090 to the Great
Public Institution.
THE Academy of Sciences received unexpectedly yesterday a gift of $10,000,
to be applied in advancing the work of the great scientific institute. John
W. Hendrie of this city was the donor. The money was given outright.
there being no suggestions as to the manner in which it should be ap
plied That will be, left to the discretion of the trustees of the academy. There
are many directions in which the fund can be used to advantage, but It will
require some consideration to determine to just what use it will be applied.
The matter will be considered at the next meeting of the trustees, when Mr.
Hendrie will be asked to participate in the discussion. The donor Is an old
Callfornian. having arrived here in early days. At present, owing to business
interests, he divides his time between this State and the East.

DRINK CHANGED HIM.
Duden Makes a Good Husband While
Sober, but Otherwise He Is
a Bad Man.
Roland H. Duden appeared before Judge
Graham yesterday to answer a charge of
disturbing the peace preferred against
him by Miss H. Kruse, who keeps a room
ing house, at 816 A Larkin street.. â¢ Mrs.
Duden lives in her house, and she com
plained that Duden called there Tuesday
morning and demanded that some wash
ing should be handed over to him. He be
came very abusive and raised a disturb
ance. -The Judge after talking to the par
ties .suggested that they should try and
settle their differences and continued the
case for ten days.
About an hour later John Harville, 22
Montgomery street, swore to a complaint
In Judge Graham's court for the arrest of
Duden on a charge of threats to kill.
Harville alleged that Duden called at his
office yesterday morning and threatened
to fill him full of lead. Duden charged
him with instigating his wife to bring a
suit for divorce against him, which he
denied.. Mrs. Duden told Judge Graham
that her husband was all right until he
got drink in him and then he was a
changed man. ';.><&;â â >. .-"â¢-",

The Mission Democratic Federation of
Primary Clubs was organized last night
at Anderson's Hall, 708 Douglas street,
The following officers were elected: Ed
ward 1.. Coffey, president; F. E. Hackney,
vice president; James A. Cotter, treas
urer; A. S.LHHe, secretary. The federa

sembly districts. The meeting was ad
dressed by Major McKlnne, who stated

mary District clubs will send delegates to
the next meeting.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The pressure has fallen steadily over Nevada
and Utah. The temperature has fallen over
I Central and Northern California about four
degrees. It has risen about twelve ' degrees
ever Utah.
Throughout California rather cold weather
prevails, the temperature ranging from two to
eight degrees below the normal.
No rain has fallen In California south of
Eureka, at which place there were light show
ers early Wednesday.
The following maximum wind velocities are
reported. Winnemucca, thirty-four miles per
hour, from the northwest; Independence, thir
ty, from the northwest, and Idaho Falls, thir
ty, from the south.
Forecasts made at San Francisco for thirty
hours ending midnight. May 18:
Northern Californiaâ Cloudy Thursday with
fog in the morning; fresh westerly winds.
Southern Californiaâ Fair Thursday with fog
in the morning; northwesterly winds.
Nevada- Thursday.
Utah â Cloudy probably showers Thursday;
colder.
Arizonaâ Cloudy; colder Thursday In north
ern portion: fair in southern portion.
San Francisco and vicinityâ Cloudy Thursday,
with fog in the morning; northwest winds.

EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK. May 17.â When it was an
nounced on Monday morning that some of the
most powerful financial Interests of the coun
try had banded themselves together for the
euppcrt of prices in the stock market, It dis
sipated very largely the timidity of the hold
ers of the group of unties which had been
so marvelously increased in price under the
favoring activities of the late ex-Governor
Flower. It also caused the professional opera
tors, who always stand ready to attack values
of securities at any vulnerable point, to pause
in their purpose and cover short contracts left
outstanding since Saturday. The capitalists
behind the Flower group apparently felt so
much encouraged with the firmness and con
fidence displayed by the market since the crisis
that they made up their minds to-day to com
plete its rehabilitation and to put prices back
to where they were on Friday last. It was
not until the latter part of the day that this
purpose seemed to be seriously in hand. In
fact, the Flower stocks as a group were con
spicuous during the early part of the day.
Their position seemed to be the natural course
of events and the general trend of the mar
ket. It was the general trend of the market
that seemed to confirm this purpose of finan
cial leaders. These events were a very marked
disinclination on the part of personal hold
ers of securities to proceed with any liquida
tion and a quiet and persistent outside de
mand for a wide range of stocks. Some very
large transactions In bonds, especially In re
cent or contemplated Issues of railroads of
the 3 and 4 per cent grades, were also in evi
dence in the pressing demand for investment
from a large reserve of capital. The money
market was notably easy and advices from
different points In the interior were of large
funds on hand. The statements of St. Paul's
earnings for the second week In May show
ing an Increase of over $24,000, compared with
the corresponding week last year, when the
extraordinary grain movement Incident to the
Lelter deal was In progress, was taken to
Indicate a continued prosperous condition of
railroad traffic. London also showed a fav
orable disposition toward American railroads
and advance. 1 prices before the opening here.
And although part of the purchases were re
sold later, having some possible strength In
the rate of exchange In London, the sales were
not in sufficient volume to threaten the money
market lure. More mature consideration of the
trade statistics for April disclosed the con
tinued strong position of our International com
merce, notwithstanding comparisons at some
points with th- unprecedented period of last
year. The market gradually but persistently
hardened under these Influences early In the
day. Central and Southern Pacific, Chicago
Terminal, the Iron and- steel shares. Sugar
and Tobacco and New York municipal corpora
tion stocks advancing sharply. In the late
afternoon the mamet leaders undertook to push
up Brooklyn Transit to where it was last
Friday. This they did through a flood of
offerings, and held it there to the close, seven
points above yesterday's close. People's Gas,
the Federal Steel stocks. International Paper,
Rock Island and Atchison preferred are from
one to four points higher. The railroads shared
very generally In the strength, the .lose be
ing buoyant and at the top.
The large demand for bonds sought persist
ently for pretty much all issues not already at
prohibitive prices. Total sales. $4,890,000. Unit
ed States new 4s and the old 4s registered
advanced 4 in the bid price.

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
I CHICAGO, May Reports of serious dam-
age to growing wheat by bugs and files put
wheat traders In a bullish mood at the start,
and with the exception of a slight dip early on
realizing the market ruled strong throughout
the session. Statements that California would
yield only half a crop attracted a good deal of
attention. July opened %c higher at 714 c, de-
clined to 71c, advanced to 72% c, and closed at
7140714 c, an advance on the day of %o%c.
The cash demand was slow and receipts were
light.
Corn declined under liquidation owing to fa-
vorable en* news and the embarrassed condi
tion of the cash trade. July declined %o%c.
Prospects of Bmall deliveries before August
strengthened oats, but the market finally weak-
ened In sympathy with corn, July closing a
shade higher.
Liberal -receipts of hogs and the weakness in
corn handicapped provisions. Shipments
small. Pork left off with a loss of 10@174c and
lard and ribs lost 2405 c each.

BOSTON WOOL MARKET.
BOSTON. May 17.â The American Wool and
Cotton Reporter will say to-morrow; The
wool market continues strong and active. The
featured of the week's business has been the
continued large demand for export, principally
for Australian wools in bond and for the Ter
ritories. The movement in the latter bids fair
t o assume large proportions, unless the holders
of such wool raise their asking prices to higher
figures than foreigners will pay. Already,
however, several million pounds of Territory
have been sold for export. About 600,000 pounds
are reported as having been sold In Phila
delphia last week to Germany, the wool costing
the purchaser 45c clean, while additional lines
have been taken here during the past week.
Prices are strong. The country markets are
excited.
The sales of the week In Boston amount to
4,113.000 pounds domestic and 4,200.600 foreign,
making a total of 8,315,600, against a total of
7,598,000 for the previous week and a total of
1,402,500 for the corresponding week last year.
Sales since January 1. 1899, amounted to 89,
- pounds, against 46,008,310 pounds last
year at this time.
LONDON .WOOL PALES.
LONDON. May 17.â The wool auction sales
were continued to-day with a large number of
buyers present. A selection of New Zealand
merino, the best offered in some time, was in
strong demand as were also some fine cross
breds. Both of these grades easily realized
the best prices of last week, the Yorkshire
representatives securing the bulk. The offer-
ings numbered 11.S00 bales.

THE STOCK MARKET.
Yesterday morning's changes .in securities
were toward lower prices. Hawaiian Commer
cial declined to $110, Hutchinson Plantation to
$34, Contra Costa Water to $68 75, Oceanic to
$89 37% and Giant Powder to $75. Other changes
were unimportant. -
With several exceptions mining stocks were
still weaker. The telegram from the pump said:
"We stopped the second elevator at 12:30 yes-
terday because of a -break In 'the water com
pany's pipe line, which" will' be repaired this
morning. Expect to start both elevators be-
fore 10 o'clock this morning and lower the
water quickly. Water rose M feet In the shaft
since 12:30 yesterday." , '- ' . "â .'.'.
The Mountain Mill and Mining Company, of
Nevada County has levied an assessment of
4 cents per share, delinquent June 2.
â  The Crown Point assessment of 10 cents per
share " will be delinquent .- In the company's
office to-day.
An official letter from the Justice mine says:
During the past week we have : been . engaged
in extracting ore and in â  work, preparatory to
Moping, and have, hoisted 92 tons and 1365
pounds of ore, car samples of which average:
Gold, $14 32; ' silver, 4.3 ounces; total. $16 91 per
ton. The Washoe mill has been running stead
ily since last report and has worked 119 tons of
ore. Owing to insufficiency of water, caused
by defects in the mill pipes, the maximum
quantity of ore was not milled last week/These
defects have . now been remedied, and we ex-
pect' henceforth to work the. mill to its : full
capacity. -" â¢ i

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the United States Hydrographic Â»
Office, located In the Merchants' Exchange, is
maintained In San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners, without regard to nationality and
free of expense. -:.--'.-
Navigators are cordially invited to visit the
office, where complete sets of charts and sail
ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
Information can always be obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation and all matters
of interest to- ocean commerce.
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building, at the foot of Market steret, is hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon, 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from, the United States Naval
Observatory. -Mare Island. Cal. â¢
A notice stating whether the ball was dropped
on time or giving the error, if any. Is published
In the morning papers the following day.
C. G. CALKINS.
â  ' Lieutenant. U. S.'N.. In charge.
TIME BALL.
Branch Hydrographic Office. U. S. N.,- Mer-
chants' Exchange, San Francisco, Cal..
April 17, 1899. .
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building was dropped at exactly noon to-dayâ
1. c., at noon of the 120 th meridian, or at 8
o'clock p. m. Greenwich time.
C. G. CALKINS.
Lieutenant. U. S. N.. in charge.
SUN. MOON AND TIDE.
United States Coast- and Geodetic Surveyâ
Times and Heights of High and Low
' Waters at Fort Point, entrance to San
Francisco Bay. Published by official au
thority of the Superintendent.
NOTEâ high and low waters occur at
the city front (Mission-street wharf) about
twenty-five minutes later, than at Fort Point;
the height of tide is the same at both places.
NOTEâIn the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given in the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day in the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
the day. the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column gives the
last tide of the day. except when there are hut
three tide?, as sometimes occur. The heights
given are additions to the soundings on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â) precedes the height.
and then the number given is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference Is the mean of the lower low waters.

from Antwerp.JAPANESE
ARE CLASSED
AS WHITES
Not Segregated on the
School Rolls.
â -â¦ â
NO IDEA OF THEIR NUMBER
â Â« â
THEY PAY NO TUITION FEE AND
HAVE ALL PRIVILEGES.
Â»
One School Has Turned Away White
Children for Lack of Room While
Japanese Were Occu
pying Desks.
i
There is no toiling by th<> figures of the
re is no toilinp: by the figures of the
school census just how many Japanese
are enjoying the benefit of free entry into
the schools of this city. They are not j
classed as Mongolians, and thai is the I
"nly class distinction made. Should a |
class he composed of forty-two whites and
three Japanese children Ii is carried on
the books of the department as a class of
forty-five. They are not separated In any
way, either In the schoolroom or in the I
statistics of the board, and so there in
be no more than a guess, and that a
very rough one, at the number now at
tending the public schools.
The department has never taken any ac
tion In regard to the little brown men
except on one occasion, when the Super
intendent tried to collect a tuition fee
from each, Under the law, non-residents
must pay a certain fee every ear for the i
privilege of attending the city schools,
and it was contended by the Superintend
ent of Schools that the Japanese pupils
were non-residents. As soon as this deci
sion was made known and the lirst at
tempt was made to collect the fee the I
missionaries who worked among the Jap
anese, here commenced to oppose the
proposition. It was claimed that the
Japanese attending the schools were resi
dents, as they were under the guardian- !
ship of residents and had severed all con
nection with the land of their birth. The
light made against the proposition caused
I
it to he laid aside, and now the Japanese !
have an equal chance with the white chil
dren of the city. .*..',>''' "
< >ver 100 is the smallest estimate any
one in authority at the office of the School
Board would put upon the number now
in the schools. Of this number the greater
portion attend the night schools, as nearly
all of them work in the day time. As a
rule, they are. pood pupils. They attend
strictly to business and there has never
been a complaint to the board from
teacher, classmate or parent. In the
Washington Evening School there are
twelve and In the Lincoln Evening School
there are fifteen, while the other evening
schools will show less than those num
bers In each. â  .:*. :.Â»--.â -.
In the day schools ten or twelve would
be considered a good umber, and it is
(nut often the figure rises so high, but
the Japanese have been becoming mora
numerous- as the y*ars pass, and where;
a few years ago a Japanese scholar
caused remark, now he is not thought of.
The greatest complaint against the. free
dom of the schools for the Japanese
comes from the Western Addition, where
the schools are already very much crowd
ed, and it frequently happens that white
scholars have been turned away for lack .
of room while at the same time there '
were attending th.- same school several
Japanese pupils, none of whom were pay
ing a cent of tuition; This is reported
to have happened out at the Pacific
Heights School in more than one in
stance.
It is urged, too, that numbers of Jap
anese far beyond the legal school age are
attending the public schools; they have
given their age as being much less than
it is and so come in with younger chil
dren. There re many full-grown Jap
anese who are now attending the pri
mary classes with children of from 6 to
10 years old.
Superintendent Webster says he Is will
ing to make every Japanese who .aims
he is under legal guardianship In this
city prove that fact or pay the regular
tuition fee. He Is also willing to ascer
tain the exact number and standing of
the Japanese on the school rolls, and he
â will probably set about this at once in
response to a request to that effect from
the president of the Western Addition
Improvement Club, the organization
â¢which started the present agitation.
The Chinese have a school of their own
and they have to attend that school or
no other, and the only way to exclude the
Japanese.l; in the opinion of the Superin
tendent, is to pass a similar law restrict
ing all Japanese to certain schools.

A Miraculous Escap
August neiimuth, a boy aged 14 year
residing at 3416 Mission street, had a m
raculous escape yesterday. The boy was
employed on a house on Albion avenue
â¢where a roof was being repaired when
he walked backward to the â :â ,â  and fell
a distance of fourteen feet. in his de
scent he turned a complete somersault
and landed on his feet. The shock, how
ever, stunned him, and Officer M. Ander
son had him conveyed to the City and
County Hospital, where Dr. Lynch "found
that a sprained ankle was the extent of
his Injuries.

WELL COOKED FOOD.
Practically every one wants some
sort of cereal for breakfast, but the
trouble of cooking the regular oatmeal
is something, and in the majority of
cases it is sticky, pasty and too much
like raw starch.
Grape-Nuts offer the ideal breakfast
food, for they require no cooking what-
ever, being thoroughly cooked at the
factory, and are therefore ready for
instant service. The food is cooked by
experts and the family does not have
to depend on the cook's ability or tem-
per for the quality of the morning
dish. Grape-Nuts are pre-digested and
suit the weakest as well as the strong-
est stomach. Sold by grocers and made
by the Postum Co., Battle Creek, Mich.
Price, 15 cents per package.-

DR. BAZET
HONORED
DR. LOUIS BAZET, the well-known
physician of this city, head of the
San Francisco Polyclinic, and a man
whose position in the front rank of
local practitioners is an acknowledged
fact, yesterday received a letter from the
Government of Portugal, Informing him
that he had been appointed by the King
a Knight of the' Ancient Military Order of
Jesus Christ. This is an exceedingly rare
honor to be conferred upon a' foreigner
and is made, so the letter states. In ac
knowledgment of Dr. Bazet's kindness to
the Portuguese residents of San Fran
cisco. The Portuguese Consul, Ignacio
da Costa Duarte, has been instructed to
confer with Dr. Bazet and arrange for the
forma! investment of the physician with
the insignia of the order.
Some time ago the Portuguese residents
of San Francisco forwarded a petition to
Lisbon, calling the attention of the King
to the services of the doctor and asking
that he be honored with some mark of
distinction as a "fitting reward. The
royal decree making him a knight of the.
grand old order, which was founded in
1317, is the answer of the King to the pe
tition of his former subjects.
Visit Vichy Springs Sunday, May 21, for
on that day tie-re will a.- an excursion
to Ukiah at only $2 for to.- round trip,
leaving Tlburon ferry at 8:30 a. m. *
THE NATIONAL UNION.
Entertainment in Celebration of the
Eighteenth Anniversary of Its
Foundation.
Under the auspices of the officers and !
delegates to the San Francisco Cabinet
of the National' Union, the seven councils
of that order last evening celebrated the I
eighteenth anniversary or the foundation
of the organization by a grand enter-
tainment in Odd Fellows' Hall. Those
who availed themselves of the Invitations
that were issued filled- the hall to its ut-
most limit, and there were many hun-
dreds who were unable to gain admission.
The hall was most tastefully decorated
with the red. white and blue and Ameri-
can flags. The souvenir programme, em-
bellished with a figure representing Cali
fornia holding a staff from which was
displayed the American flag, was filled
with finei half-tone pictures of San Fran-
cisco views and .scenes of prominent
places in California.
After introductory remarks by Norman
R Arter. the president of the cabinet,
there was presented the following high-
Class programme of entertainment, the
several numbers being exceedingly well
received and loudly applauded:
National Union quartetâ Frederic TMrdy, Ed

contralto solo. Mrs. J. K. Birmingham; . "The

MERCHANTS PROTECTED
FROM PRYING RIVALS
OUTWARD FOREIGN MANIFESTS
TO BE GUARDED STRICTLY.
Customs Brokers and Commercial Re-
porters to Be Pledged to Secrecy
So Far as Shippers; Names
Are Concerned.
Owing 'to the fact that for the past
twenty years certain, unscrupulous per-
sons have been examining outward foreign
manifests In the Custom House for the
purpose of prying into the secrets of busi-
ness rivals, Customs Collector Jackson
authorized Deputy Collector Farley yes-
terday to lsue the following order:
From and after this date no person except-
ing the broker who clears a vessel and his
employe who prepares the outward foreign
j manifest and such customs employes only as
j are required in the performance of "their of
: ficial duties to handle the Fame, will he per
| ml ted as heretofore to use the shippers' mani
! fests to prepare outward foreign manifests, but
( they and their employes are cautioned not to
j divulge nor reveal the marks of the consignees
or the names of shippers, and all shippers'
; manifests in their possession must be tran
, scribed on the outward foreign manifests and
returned to the entrance and clearing clerks
before clearance of the vessel will be granted.
Representatives of newspapers, when they
l shall have signed a stipulation not to publish,
j divulge nor reveal the marks (or names of
1 shippers), if learned accidentally or otherwise
i from the custom house records, will be per
-1 mitted to examine the outward foreign mani
| fests for the purpose of preparing statistics
"of exports. This stipulation Is now In pos-
session of Colonel, Craig and must be signed
t before said outward foreign manifests may be
; examined.
Other persons making a specialty of any par
ticular line of trade who desire to prepare
statistics of exports concerning such trade must
make written application to the undersigned
statins? the particular statistics they desire and
stipulating that they will not divulge any in
formation that might lead to the names of
consignees or shippers. N. S. FARLEY, Dep
uty Collector.
MIDWEEK NOTES OF
THE THEATER WORLD
Charles Frohman's production of "On
and Off" has only until Sunday night to
run at the Columbia, giving way to the
Kelcey-Shannon company in "The Moth
and the Flame."
Clay Greene's new farce, "His Japanese
Wife," is concluding a successful fort
night at the Alcazar, where It will ho
replaced on Monday night by Augustus
Thomas' great local color play, "In Miz
zoura." with Ernest Hastings ln the part
created by Nat Goodwin.
The unusual double bill of "Pinafore"
and "Cavalleria Rustincana" has made a
strong hit at the Grand. Next week the
Southwell company willing "The Gypsy
Baron." ,- __.'*

Koven and Smith, is announced to fol
low.
The acrobats are in the lead at the Or
pheum this week. The six Montroses give
a wonderful exhibition of tumbling and
the Escamillos do prodigious feats on the
tight wire. - . .

and the special event is another cake
walk of all nations.

Flourished a Knife. V
* William Thompson, a. fireman, drew a
knife in a saloon on Fourth street, near
Howard, last evening, and after flourish
ing it above his head threatened to carve
the bartender. Tie was arrested after a
short struggle- and charged at the. City
Prison with exhibiting a deadly weapon
in a rude manner. .

INSPECTION
OF SCHOOLS
CONCLUDED
Appropriation for Re-
pairs Will Be Asked.
, â â
POOR SANITARY CONDITION
â â
LACK OF FUNDS TO FILL THE
PRESENT NEEDS.
. Â»
Mayor Phelan Evinces an Interest
and Spoke Words of Condem-
nation for Some Things
That He Saw.
Jâ+.t '
The tour of inspection by" Mayor
Pip lan, city officers and members of the
Board of Education, for the purpose of
examining into the condition of school
houses and accessory buildings, was con
tinued and. concluded yesterday. During
the morning several dispensaries . of
learning were visited and In each place
the sanitary conditions and stability of
the buildings were noted, and will be em
bodied in a special report which will be
presented to the Supervisors in the form
of a recommendation for an appropria
tion with which to repair dilapidated
school property and place it in a state
conducive to health.
The idea of inviting the Mayor and
officials outside of the .school depart
ment, to view for themselves' the neces
sity of additional funds for the attention
of property which is fast going to wreck
was conceived by George R. Armstrong,
chairman of the Buildings and Grounds
Committee. Accordingly, he arranged for
the transportation of the inspectors, and
on Tuesday a large part of the school
territory of the city was covered. Yes
terday the party, composed of Mayor
Phelan, Auditor Wells, W. R. Sherwood
of the Merchants' Association, C. A.
Murdock, foreman of the Grand Jury,
Supervisor Deasv, President Bergerot :of
the Board of Education. Architect Welch.
Inspector George Walker, Assistant In
spector XV. E. Commary and Directors
Kemp, -Hoi brook, Armstrong, Gedge,
Brenaenstein, Conlon and Stohl, again
took leave of the City Hall. .
The first place visited was the Frank
lin Grammar School, after which the
Buena Vista School was inspected. , The
Height Primary, Which was condemned
four years ago, was next on the list, and
then the party journeyed to the Fair
mount Primary, then to Monroe Primary,
Mission Grammar and Marshall Primary.
In every school visited, with the excep
tion of one,' sanitary conditions were ex
ceedingly poor, and in many cases dan
gerous to the health of children. The
sheds, fences, outbuildings and plank
walks were sadly in need of repairs, and
theseÂ« things were pointed out to the
members of the party.
Mayor Phelan took a number of notes
and asked a few questions. He often ex
pressed disgust at some things which ex
isted, but was non-committal on the sub
ject of a general remedy. He is in favor.
however, of attending to the wants of
health immediately. The total amount
asked for by the board borders on $350,
--000, and will" include the construction of
several new buildings. Some of the
schools are crowded to nearly twice their.
capacity, and It has been proposed to
either raise them one story or to build
wings on either side. The amount will 1> â¢
Included In the next tax levy, and the
recommendation that it be set aside from
public funds will be presented to tie-
Board of Supervisors at an early date.
Western Addition Club.
The Western Addition Literary and
Social Club held Its regular monthly en
tertainment last night at Native Sons'
Hall. There was the usual large at
tendance. The programme was a most
Interesting one.

BERKELEY, May 17.â Exactly 360
BERKELEY. May 37. -Exactly 360
students graduated from the University
of California to-day. They received their
diplomas at the public commencement ex
orcises held in a big tent this morning
upon the university campus.
Although 10:30 o'clock had been set as
the time for beginning the graduating
ceren\ony, the friends of the candidates
for degrees 'axed the big tent to Its ut
most capacity long before the hour ar
rived. Hundreds of people from San
Francisco and Oakland had to he con
tent with standing outside. A large plat
form had been erected at one end for the
president and members of the faculty.
On both sides stood chairs and seats for
the students. An elaborate display of
palms and potted plants from the univer
sity conservatory served to cover up the
bareness of the whole.
Shortly after 10 o'clock, with President
Martin Kellogg in the lead, the pro
cession of graduates, members of the
faculty and regents started from Stiles
Hall. They walked across the campus in
double file, the women dressed in white.
wearing the black cap and gown, the. men
for th.- most part In black. The scene
was full of academic mpressiveness as
the venerable president, clad In a doc
tor's gown, took his seat upon tne plat
form, followed by the members of the
Board of Regents in a body, and a ma
jority of the university professors, while
the 360 graduates arranged themselves on
either side As soon as all were seated.
the Rev. George B. Hatch of Berkeley
offered prayer.
Following was the order of 'exercises:
Festival overture. I.eutner: prayer. Rev.
George B. Hatch; statement by the president:
serenade, Titi; ..ration. 'Exituri Salutamus."
GeorKe Elliott Ebrijrht, of the medical depart
ment: oration. "Self-Sacrifice and Self-Asser
tion." Roy Victor Nye; "Album Leaf," Wag
ner; oration. "The Duties of the Legal Pro
fession In the Present Crisis." Adolph Leopold
Well. Ph.B., of the Hastings College of the
Law; oration. "The Debt the Graduate of the
University of California Owes to the State."
Miss Lily Hohfeld; Walts, "Artist's Life,"
Strauss: conferring of degrees, by the presi
dent; delivery of mllltarv commissions, by Pro
fessor Frank Soule; benediction, Rev. Horatio
Stebbins. D.D.
In his opening address President Kel
logg announced, the official statistics of
the number of students in the university.
There were, he said. 1750 In the colleges at
Berkeley and 2200 altogether, including
the Affiliated Colleges in San Francisco.
This meant an increase of 46 per cent In
ten years. The president deplored the
fact that with this unprecedented increase
in numbers the funds available for the
expenses of the institution had in the last
ten years Increased but 33 per cent. The
next year, he explained, even allowing
for every economy, would show a deficit
of 147,000. He urged the alumni and all
the friends of the university to use their
efforts in helping to lighten the financial
burden resting upon their Alma Mater.
Under the title. "Exituri Salutamus,"
George Elliott right of the medical
department delivered a brief oration in
tended to set forth the right attitude of
the physician just graduated toward the
profession of his choice. Mr. Ebright
found no-cause for alarm In the fact that
the practice of medicine was already
overcrowded. "As long as sickness and
any form of organic evil exists," he said,
"the physician will be needed; and not
until every problem of disease Is over
come may his labor cease. To this end
the physician must regard his calling as
a sacred duty. Putting pecuniary recom
pense aside, he should struggle under tho
white banner of charity, seeking no other
reward save the reward of the work It
self."
Mr. Ebright was followed by Roy Victor
Nye, president of the senior class. Mr.
Nye's oration was entitled "Self-sacrifice
and Self-assertion." He outlined briefly
the principles Involved ln each of these
two motives of conduct, and concluded
that both were necessary, that the one
must counterbalance the other In a true
realization of the rids of life.
Adolph Leopold Well, Ph.H.. spoke upon
"The Duties of the Legal Profession in
the Present Crisis." Mr. Well is about to
enter the legal profession, after getting
through the Hastings College of Law.
His opinions about expansion and im
perialism are decidedly in opposition to
these policies, and he accordingly con
cluded that the legal profession ought to
think that way too. Chief Justice Mar
shall was all wrong, he declared, in many
of his decisions, and particularly in all
those which have been construed In favor
of the present policy of the Government.
Miss Lily Hohfeld, the university
medalist for the present year, delivered
the last oration. The title was "The Debt
the Graduate of the University of Cali
fornia Owes the State." "Free education
on the part of the State," she said, "was
an implied promise to give something
back in return. The college graduate has
a two-fold duty: he must help in the
shaping of a rational and Intelligent pub
lic opinion, and he must embody in his
personal life the ideals of his own na
ture."
At the conclusion of Miss Hohfeld's ad
dress President Kollogg proceeded with
the awarding of diplomas. The candi
dates for degrees from the different col
leges were asked to stand while their
names were read off and tho diplomas
were handed to them. In the absence of
Governor Gage, Professor Frank Soule,
commandant of the University Corps of
Cadets, delivered the military commis
sions to the cadet officers. After the Rev.
Horatio Stebbins of San Francisco had
pronounced a benediction the ceremonies
concluded.
A full list of all the graduates, together
with the military commissions and the
names of those to whom scholarships
have been granted for the ensuing year,
was published ln The Call of to-day.
Following the exercises came the annual
alumni reunion and luncheon ln the Har
mon Gymnasium. After luncheon had
been served the following programme,
consisting of speeches by alumni, was
carried out:
Welcome, Professor William E. Hitter, .'BB.
president of the Associated Alumni; "The
Faculty Point of View." President Martin Kel
logg; music, selections, "Carmen" (Bizet);
"Recent Developments in Higher Education,"
Professor John Dewey; "Thirty Tears. ln the
Vineyard," Professor Frank Soule; music,
overture, "National," United States (Gilmore);
"Co-education Then and Now," Mrs. Alex

Blessed Them in Her Will.
OAKLAND, May 17.â olographic
Will of Elizabeth Whcaton was filed for
probate to-day by her son, George H.
Wheaton, who asks for letters and who
is named as executor. The Instrument is
dated September, 1889. and leaves every
thing to the six children, and expresses
the wish that the present homestead at
1060 Poplar street remain so declared for
the next ten years. The value of the es
tate Is not known.. In the concluding
clause of the will Mrs. Wheaton leaves
this blessing: "That an overruling Prov
idence will guide and protect you, and the
spirit of love, peace and good will abide
with you all Is the heartfelt prayer of
your mother."
YOUNGEST DENTIST
IN CALIFORNIA
DR. ARTHUR L. IA/HITE.
AMONG the graduates of the College of Dentistry of the University of
California at Berkeley yesterday was Dr. Arthur Loring White, who
had attained his majority just long enough, ago to allow him to re-
ceive his diploma at the end of this term. Although the youngest
dentist In the State, his work in operative dentistry Is rated with the best of
his class.
DEFECTIVE PLUMBING
IN THE CITY HALL
REPORT MADE TO THE BOARD
OF HEALTH.
The Establishment of a Hospital in
This City for Chinese Is Con-
sidered Desirable.
_ â _ â . â
At the regular meeting of the Board of
At the regular meeting of the Board of
Health held last evening Secretary Dunne
reported that some complaints bad been
made regarding the performance of au
topsies at the City and County Hospital.
On motion of Dr. Williamson a resolu
tion was adopted Instructing the superin
tendent of the hospital to' refuse requests
for autopsies In all cases where the cause
of death is apparent. '
Plumbing Inspectors Sullivan. Kelly
and Ward made an exhaustive report on
the sanitary condition of the City Hall
relative to the plumbing in the building.
The report shows that some of the
plumbing has been In place almost twenty
years and Is now practically worthless.
The condition of the plumbing in the City
Prison. Auditor's office and the office of
the Assessor is reported as being partic- I
ularly bad. Many of the drain pipes lead
ing from the roof of the building to the
sewers have been broken or are otherwise i
defective, so that the water runs direct
into the sub-basement. Some of these
pipes have evidently been stolen, as many !
joints are missing and cannot be found. !
Particular attention is given the plumb- ,
ing in the City Prison, which is out of
date and wholly Inadequate to the needs
of the institution. The report was re- .
ferred to the Buildings Committe of the
Board of Supervisors.
The charge of incivility ' preferred
against Assistant Secretary Zobel some
time ago by Mrs. White was dismissed,
there being insufficient evidence to sup
port the charge.^gpiSEax
The board by unanimous consent In
dorsed the proposition for the establish
ment of a Chinese hospital where all Chi
nese cases other than those of a con
tagious nature may be gathered under
one. roof, where their treatment may be '
supervised by American physicians.
The resignation of W. Cavelry, Inspec- |
tor of Garbage, was received and ac
cepted. W. Greeg was appointed to the
vacancy,
A communication was received from
General Shafter. expressing a desire on
his part to assist the board In any way
within his power in the matter of quar
antine affairs and explaining that the af
fairs of the Federal quarantine were
without his jurisdiction.

INTERNAL REVENUE RULINGS.
Gift Conveyances Taxableâ Witch
' .Hazel' Is Toilet Water.
The Commissioner of Internal Revenue
has decided that all gift conveyances of
real estate are subject to taxation, and
the tax Is based upon the value of the
real estate conveyed by the Instrument,
and this value is to be found as though
the property was unincumbered, if it is
incumbered. When a husband desires to
convey property to his wife for the pur
pose of a gift, and the property is con
veyed to a third party, and by said third
party conveyed to the wife, both deeds
are subject to a taxation based on the
value of the property, conveyed.
Another decision rescinds the former.
ruling holding witch hazel taxable only !
THE SAIS FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, MAY 18, 1899.
when it is held out as a specific for some
ailment or put up In the style and man
ner of proprietary medicines, and it is
held instead to be taxable under schedule
11 as a toilet water, must be stamped in
bulk or otherwise before it Is removed
from the place of manufacture or impor
tation, regardless of the style or manner
In which it is sold or what claims are
made for it on the label, or ln fact
whether it Is labeled at all or not, so long
as the fact that it is witch hazel is
known.

HARES WILL SUFFER.
Dogs That Will Run to Kill at Union
Park on Saturday and Sunday.
Owing to the exasperating delays that
1 are occasioned in the journey to and from
Union Coursing Park when traveling on
the steam cars coursing enthusiasts are
now taking advantage of the quick trips
that are made from this city by the San
: Francisco and San Mateo Electric Rail
road direct to the grounds. The fare is
i only 10 cents, and the ride on the electric
i road is far more pleasant than the jour
ney on the steam cars, which make . sev
eral unnecessary stops to allow freight
and passenger trains the right of way.
The ieashmen are now patronizing the
electric road because of its superior ac
commodations.
